<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Hawker Hurricane</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><table class="infobox" style="width:315px;border-spacing:2px;">
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align: center; font-size: large; padding-bottom: 0.3em;">Hurricane</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg"><img alt="Hurricane mk1 r4118 fairford arp.jpg" data-file-height="1501" data-file-width="2308" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg/300px-Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg/450px-Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg/600px-Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></td>

<p>The <b>Hawker Hurricane</b> is a British single-seat <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighter aircraft</a> of the 1930s–1940s that was designed and predominantly built by <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Aircraft" title="Hawker Aircraft">Hawker Aircraft</a> Ltd for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF). Although overshadowed by the <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire" title="Supermarine Spitfire">Supermarine Spitfire</a>, the aircraft became renowned during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a>, accounting for 60 percent of the RAF air victories in the battle, and served in all the major theatres of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>.</p>
<p>The Hurricane originated from discussions during the early 1930s between RAF officials and British aircraft designer <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Camm" title="Sydney Camm">Sir Sydney Camm</a> on the topic of a proposed <a href="/wiki/Monoplane" title="Monoplane">monoplane</a> derivative of the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Fury" title="Hawker Fury">Hawker Fury</a> <a href="/wiki/Biplane" title="Biplane">biplane</a>. Despite an institutional preference for biplanes at the time and repeated lack of interest by the <a href="/wiki/Air_Ministry" title="Air Ministry">Air Ministry</a>, Hawker chose to continue refining their monoplane proposal, which resulted in the incorporation of several innovations that would become critical to wartime fighter aircraft, such as a retractable <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">undercarriage</a> and the newly developed <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">Rolls-Royce Merlin</a> engine. In late 1934, the Air Ministry placed an order for Hawker's <i>"Interceptor Monoplane"</i>. On 6 November 1935, the prototype Hurricane, <i>K5083</i>, performed its <a href="/wiki/Maiden_flight" title="Maiden flight">maiden flight</a>.</p>
<p>In June 1936, the Hurricane was ordered into production by the Air Ministry; the type entered squadron service on 25 December 1937. The manufacture and maintenance of the aircraft was greatly eased by its use of conventional construction methods, which enabled squadrons to perform many major repairs themselves without much external support. The Hurricane was rapidly procured prior to the outbreak of the Second World War in September 1939, by which point, the RAF operated a total of 18 Hurricane-equipped squadrons. The aircraft quickly found itself being heavily relied upon to defend against the vast and varied German aircraft operated by <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Dogfight" title="Dogfight">dogfighting</a> with the capable <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109">Messerschmitt Bf 109</a>, across multiple theatres of action. It is perhaps best known for its contribution to Britain's home defences during the Battle of Britain.</p>
<p>The Hurricane evolved through several versions and adaptations, resulting in a series of aircraft which acted as fighters, bomber-interceptors, fighter-bombers (also called "Hurribombers") and <a href="/wiki/Close_air_support" title="Close air support">ground support</a> aircraft. Further navalised versions, which were popularly known as the <b>Sea Hurricane</b>, received modifications that enabled their operation from ships; some of these were converted to be used as catapult-launched convoy escorts, known as "Hurricats". By the end of production in 1944, in excess of 14,583 Hurricanes had been completed (including at least 800 that had been converted to a Sea Hurricane configuration<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thetford_1994.2C_p._232_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thetford_1994.2C_p._232-3">[3]</a></sup> and around 1,400 that had been constructed in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> by <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Car_and_Foundry" title="Canadian Car and Foundry">Canadian Car and Foundry</a>).</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the era in which the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Aircraft" title="Hawker Aircraft">Hawker Aircraft</a> company would develop the Hurricane, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fighter_Command" title="RAF Fighter Command">RAF Fighter Command</a> consisted of just 13 squadrons, each equipped with either the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Fury" title="Hawker Fury">Hawker Fury</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawker_Demon" title="Hawker Demon">Hawker Demon</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Bulldog" title="Bristol Bulldog">Bristol Bulldog</a>, all <a href="/wiki/Biplane" title="Biplane">biplanes</a> furnished with fixed-<a href="/wiki/Blade_pitch" title="Blade pitch">pitch</a> wooden propellers and non-retractable undercarriages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bader_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bader-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_3_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_3-5">[5]</a></sup> At the time, there was an institutional reluctance towards change within the <a href="/wiki/Air_Staff_(United_Kingdom)" title="Air Staff (United Kingdom)">Air Staff</a>; according to aviation author Francis K. Mason, some senior figures were prejudiced against the adoption of <a href="/wiki/Monoplane" title="Monoplane">monoplane</a> fighter aircraft, while mid-level officers were typically approachable on the subject and design concepts that made use of such configurations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_3_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-mason_3-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1934, the British <a href="/wiki/Air_Ministry" title="Air Ministry">Air Ministry</a> issued <a href="/wiki/List_of_Air_Ministry_specifications#1930.E2.80.931939" title="List of Air Ministry specifications">Specification F.7/30</a> in response to demands within the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF) for a new generation of <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighter aircraft</a>. Earlier, during 1933, British aircraft designer <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Camm" title="Sydney Camm">Sydney Camm</a> had conducted discussions with Major John Buchanan of the Directorate of Technical Development on a monoplane based on the existing Fury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_3_4_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_3_4-6">[6]</a></sup> Mason attributes Camm's discussions with figures within the RAF, such as Squadron Leader <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Sorley" title="Ralph Sorley">Ralph Sorley</a>, as having provoked the specification and some of its details, such as the preference for armaments being installed within the wings instead of within the aircraft's nose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_4_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_4-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Camm's initial submission in response to F.7/30, the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_P.V.3" title="Hawker P.V.3">Hawker P.V.3</a>, was essentially a scaled-up version of the Fury biplane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> However, the P.V.3 was not among the proposals which the Air Ministry had selected to be constructed as a government-sponsored prototype. After the rejection of the P.V.3 proposal, Camm commenced work upon a new design involving a <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilever</a> monoplane arrangement, complete with a fixed undercarriage, armed with four machine guns and powered by the <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Goshawk" title="Rolls-Royce Goshawk">Rolls-Royce Goshawk</a> engine. The original 1934 armament specifications for what would evolve into the Hurricane were for a similar armament fitment to the <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Gladiator" title="Gloster Gladiator">Gloster Gladiator</a>: four machine-guns, two in the wings and two in the fuselage, <a href="/wiki/Synchronization_gear" title="Synchronization gear">synchronized</a> to fire through the propeller arc. By January 1934, the proposal's detail drawings had been finished, but these failed to impress the Air Ministry enough for a prototype to be ordered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Camm's response to this rejection was to further develop the design, during which a retractable undercarriage was introduced and the unsatisfactory Goshawk engine was replaced by a new Rolls-Royce design, initially designated as the <i>PV-12</i>, which would subsequently go on to become famous as the <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">Merlin</a> engine. In August 1934, a one-tenth scale model of the design was produced and dispatched to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Physical_Laboratory,_UK" title="National Physical Laboratory, UK">National Physical Laboratory</a> at <a href="/wiki/Teddington" title="Teddington">Teddington</a>. A series of <a href="/wiki/Wind_tunnel" title="Wind tunnel">wind tunnel</a> tests confirmed the aerodynamic qualities of the aircraft were in order, and in September 1934, Camm again approached the Air Ministry. This time, the Ministry's response was favourable, and a prototype of the "Interceptor Monoplane" was promptly ordered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prototype_and_trials">Prototype and trials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prototype and trials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_mockup.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="999" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hurricane_mockup.jpg/220px-Hurricane_mockup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hurricane_mockup.jpg/330px-Hurricane_mockup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hurricane_mockup.jpg/440px-Hurricane_mockup.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_mockup.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An early mock-up for the Hurricane's fuselage, showing side fuselage-mounted <a href="/wiki/Synchronization_gear" title="Synchronization gear">synchronized</a> machine gun, like earlier British biplane fighters.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In November 1934, the Air Ministry issued Specification F.5/34 which, drawing on the work of Squadron Leader Ralph Sorley, called for new fighter aircraft to be armed with a total of eight guns. However, by this time, work had progressed too far to immediately modify the planned four-gun installation. By January 1935, a wooden mock-up had been finished, and although a number of suggestions for detail changes were made, construction of the prototype was approved, and a new specification (F.36/34) was written around the design. In July 1935, this specification was amended to include installation of eight guns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_4_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-mason_4-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of August 1935, work on the airframe, performed at Hawker's <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Kingston upon Thames">Kingston upon Thames</a> facility, had been completed and the aircraft components were transported to <a href="/wiki/Brooklands" title="Brooklands">Brooklands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a>, where Hawker had an assembly shed; on 23 October 1935, the prototype was fully re-assembled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_4_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-mason_4-7">[7]</a></sup> Ground testing and taxi trials took place over the following two weeks. On 6 November 1935, the prototype <i>K5083</i> took to the air for the <a href="/wiki/Maiden_flight" title="Maiden flight">first time</a> at the hands of Hawker's chief <a href="/wiki/Test_pilot" title="Test pilot">test pilot</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_Lieutenant" title="Flight Lieutenant">Flight Lieutenant</a> (later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_Captain" title="Group Captain">Group Captain</a>) <a href="/wiki/George_Bulman_(pilot)" title="George Bulman (pilot)">George Bulman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greatac_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-greatac-12">[12]</a></sup> Bulman was assisted by two other pilots in subsequent flight testing; <a href="/wiki/Philip_Lucas" title="Philip Lucas">Philip Lucas</a> flew some of the experimental test flights, while <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Hindmarsh" title="John Stuart Hindmarsh">John Hindmarsh</a> conducted the firm's production flight trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> As completed, the prototype had been fitted with <a href="/wiki/Ballast" title="Ballast">ballast</a> to represent the aircraft's armament prior to the acceptance of the final multi-gun wing armament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>By March 1936, a total of ten flying hours had been performed by the prototype, covering all major portions of the flight envelope; early flight testing had gone reasonably well, especially in light of the trial status of the Merlin engine, which had itself yet to achieve full flight certification at this time and thus severe restrictions had been imposed upon use of the engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_4_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-mason_4-7">[7]</a></sup> In early 1936, the prototype was transferred to <a href="/wiki/RAF_Martlesham_Heath" title="RAF Martlesham Heath">RAF Martlesham Heath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">Suffolk</a>, to participate in initial service trials under the direction of squadron leader D. F. Anderson. Sammy Wroath, later to be the founding Commandant of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empire_Test_Pilot_School" title="Empire Test Pilot School">Empire Test Pilot School</a>, was the RAF test pilot for the Hurricane: his report was favorable, stating that: "The aircraft is simple and easy to fly and has no apparent vices" and proceeded to praise its control response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_before_maiden_flight_1935.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="800" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hawker_Hurricane_before_maiden_flight_1935.jpg/220px-Hawker_Hurricane_before_maiden_flight_1935.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hawker_Hurricane_before_maiden_flight_1935.jpg/330px-Hawker_Hurricane_before_maiden_flight_1935.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hawker_Hurricane_before_maiden_flight_1935.jpg/440px-Hawker_Hurricane_before_maiden_flight_1935.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_before_maiden_flight_1935.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>K5083</i>, the prototype, photographed before its first flight in November 1935</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the course of RAF trials, despite the Merlin engine proving to be problematic, having suffered numerous failures and necessitating several changes, enthusiastic reports were produced in the aircraft and its performance figures. The trials had observed the aircraft to possess a maximum level speed of 315 MPH at an altitude of 16,200 ft, a time-to-climb to an altitude of 15,000 ft from takeoff of 5.7 minutes, and a stalling speed of 57 MPH (only marginally higher than the Gladiator biplane), the last achieved using its <a href="/wiki/Flap_(aeronautics)" title="Flap (aeronautics)">flaps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_4_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-mason_4-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the course of further testing, it was found that the Hurricane had poor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin_(flight)" title="Spin (flight)">spin</a> recovery characteristics, in which all <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudder</a> authority could be lost due to shielding of the rudder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_5_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_5-16">[16]</a></sup> Hawker's response to the issue was to request that spinning tests be waived, but the Air Ministry refused the request;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> the situation was resolved by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Aircraft_Establishment" title="Royal Aircraft Establishment">Royal Aircraft Establishment</a> (RAE), who established that the aerodynamic problem had been caused by a breakdown of the airflow over the lower fuselage, and could be cured by the addition of a small ventral <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_fairing" title="Aircraft fairing">fairing</a> and extension of the bottom of the rudder. This discovery had come too late for the changes to be incorporated in the first production aircraft, but were introduced upon the 61st built and all subsequent aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1936, the Hawker <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Board_of_Directors" title="Board of Directors">Board of Directors</a> had decided, in the absence of official authorisation and at company expense, to proceed with issuing the design drawings to the production design office and to commence tooling-up for a production line capable of producing a batch of 1,000 Hurricanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_4_5_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_4_5-20">[20]</a></sup> In June 1936, the Air Ministry issued a contract to Hawker for the manufacture of an initial batch of 600 production aircraft. On 26 June 1936, the type name <i>"Hurricane"</i>, which had been proposed by Hawker, was approved by the Air Ministry; an informal christening ceremony for the aircraft was carried out during the following month during an official visit by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Edward_VIII" title="King Edward VIII">King Edward VIII</a> to Martlesham Heath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_5_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-mason_5-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Women_assembling_Hawker_Hurricanes_1942.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="764" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Women_assembling_Hawker_Hurricanes_1942.jpg/220px-Women_assembling_Hawker_Hurricanes_1942.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Women_assembling_Hawker_Hurricanes_1942.jpg/330px-Women_assembling_Hawker_Hurricanes_1942.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Women_assembling_Hawker_Hurricanes_1942.jpg/440px-Women_assembling_Hawker_Hurricanes_1942.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Women_assembling_Hawker_Hurricanes_1942.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane production line, 1942</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Air_Force_Training_Command,_1939-1940._C851.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1772" data-file-width="2378" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Royal_Air_Force_Training_Command%2C_1939-1940._C851.jpg/220px-Royal_Air_Force_Training_Command%2C_1939-1940._C851.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Royal_Air_Force_Training_Command%2C_1939-1940._C851.jpg/330px-Royal_Air_Force_Training_Command%2C_1939-1940._C851.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Royal_Air_Force_Training_Command%2C_1939-1940._C851.jpg/440px-Royal_Air_Force_Training_Command%2C_1939-1940._C851.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Air_Force_Training_Command,_1939-1940._C851.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Trainee aircraft fitters working on instructional partly-assembled Hurricanes, circa 1939–1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In June 1936, the Hurricane was formally ordered into production, the Air Ministry having placed its first order that month for 600 aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_5_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-mason_5-16">[16]</a></sup> The Hurricane was a relatively easy aircraft to manufacture. In comparison to the <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire" title="Supermarine Spitfire">Supermarine Spitfire</a>, it was significantly cheaper and involved less labour, requiring 10,300 man hours to produce versus 15,200 for the Spitfire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-postan_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-postan-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>A key reason for the aircraft's appeal was its relatively simple construction and ease of manufacture. As a large-scale war was looking increasingly likely, and time was of the essence in providing the RAF with an effective fighter aircraft, it was unclear if the more advanced Spitfire would enter production smoothly, while the Hurricane made use of well-understood manufacturing techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-postan_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-postan-22">[22]</a></sup> This factor was equally applicable for its use within service squadrons as well, which were experienced in working on and repairing aircraft whose construction employed the same principles as the Hurricane, and the simplicity of its design enabled the improvisation of some remarkable repairs in squadron workshops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-postan_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-postan-22">[22]</a></sup> A fabric-covered wing patterned upon traditional Hawker designs was initially adopted in order to speed up production; a higher-performing stressed-skin metal wing took its place in late 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_5_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-mason_5-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 12 October 1937, the maiden flight took place of the first production Hurricane I, which was powered by a Merlin II engine and flown by flight lieutenant Philip Lucas. Production deliveries had been delayed by roughly six months due to a decision to equip the Hurricane only with the improved Merlin II engine, while the earlier Merlin I had been prioritised for the <a href="/wiki/Fairey_Battle" title="Fairey Battle">Fairey Battle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Henley" title="Hawker Henley">Hawker Henley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_5_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-mason_5-16">[16]</a></sup> By the following December, the first four aircraft to enter service with the RAF had joined <a href="/wiki/No._111_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 111 Squadron RAF">No. 111 Squadron</a>, stationed at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Northolt" title="RAF Northolt">RAF Northolt</a>. By February 1939, No. 111 Squadron had received 16 Hurricanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_5_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-mason_5-16">[16]</a></sup> Upon the outbreak of the Second World War, nearly 500 Hurricanes had been produced, which had equipped a total of 18 squadrons, while a further 3,500 aircraft were on order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_7_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_7-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 1940, <a href="/wiki/Max_Aitken,_1st_Baron_Beaverbrook" title="Max Aitken, 1st Baron Beaverbrook">Lord Beaverbrook</a>, who was the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Aircraft_Production" title="Minister of Aircraft Production">Minister of Aircraft Production</a>, established an organisation in which a number of manufacturers were seconded to repair and overhaul battle-damaged Hurricanes. The <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Repair_Organisation" title="Civilian Repair Organisation">Civilian Repair Organisation</a> also overhauled battle-weary aircraft, which were later sent to training units or to other air forces; one of the factories involved was the <a href="/wiki/Austin_Motor_Company" title="Austin Motor Company">Austin Aero Company</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Cofton_Hackett#Austin_Aero_Works" title="Cofton Hackett">Cofton Hackett plant</a>. Another was David Rosenfield Ltd, based at <a href="/wiki/Barton-upon-Irwell" title="Barton-upon-Irwell">Barton</a> aerodrome near <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>.</p>
<p>A major manufacturer of the Hurricane was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canada_Car_and_Foundry" title="Canada Car and Foundry">Canada Car and Foundry</a> at their factory in <a href="/wiki/Fort_William,_Ontario" title="Fort William, Ontario">Fort William, Ontario</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> (the facility's Chief Engineer, <a href="/wiki/Elsie_MacGill" title="Elsie MacGill">Elsie MacGill</a>, became known as the "Queen of the Hurricanes").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_7_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-mason_7-24">[24]</a></sup> The initiative was commercially led rather than governmentally, but was endorsed by the British government; Hawker, having recognised that a major conflict was all but inevitable after the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich Crisis</a> of 1938, drew up preliminary plans to expand Hurricane production via a new factory in Canada. Under this plan, samples, pattern aircraft, and a complete set of design documents stored on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microfilm" title="Microfilm">microfilm</a>, were shipped to Canada; 22 early British-produced Hurricanes were transferred to support the development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_7_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-mason_7-24">[24]</a></sup> As a result, Canadian-built Hurricanes were shipped to Britain to participate in events such as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_7_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-mason_7-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Overall, some 14,000 Hurricanes and Sea Hurricanes were produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thetford_1994.2C_p._232_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thetford_1994.2C_p._232-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="It would be nice to know how many fighters versus fighter-bombers versus other variants. It is not provided in variants section either. None-the-less, we are told a great deal (March 2011)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The majority of Hurricanes were built by Hawker (who produced the type until 1944), while Hawker's sister company, the <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Aircraft_Company" title="Gloster Aircraft Company">Gloster Aircraft Company</a>, constructed 2,750. The Austin Aero Company completed 300 Hurricanes. Canada Car and Foundry was responsible for the production of 1,400 Hurricanes, designated as the Mk X.</p>
<p>In 1939, production of 100 Hurricanes was initiated in <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a> by <a href="/wiki/Zmaj_aircraft" title="Zmaj aircraft">Zmaj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rogo%C5%BEarski" title="Rogožarski">Rogožarski</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_6_7_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_6_7-25">[25]</a></sup> Of these, 20 were built by Zmaj by April 1941. Recognising that the supply of British-made Merlin engines might not be guaranteed, it was decided to fit one of the Yugoslavian Hurricanes with a <a href="/wiki/Daimler-Benz_DB_601" title="Daimler-Benz DB 601">Daimler-Benz DB 601</a> engine instead; this aircraft was <a href="/wiki/Flight_test" title="Flight test">test flown</a> in 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_7_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-mason_7-24">[24]</a></sup> In 1938, a contract for 80 Hurricanes was placed with Fairey's Belgian subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Avions_Fairey" title="Avions Fairey">Avions Fairey SA</a> for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belgian_Air_Force" title="Belgian Air Force">Belgian Air Force</a>; it was intended to arm these aircraft with an arrangement of four 13.2 mm <a href="/wiki/M1919_Browning_machine_gun" title="M1919 Browning machine gun">Browning machine guns</a>. Three were built and two flown with this armament by the time of the <i><a href="/wiki/Blitzkrieg" title="Blitzkrieg">Blitzkrieg</a></i> in May 1940, with at least 12 more constructed by Avions Fairey armed with the conventional eight rifle calibre machine gun armament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Half_pt_4_p34_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Half_pt_4_p34-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_7_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-mason_7-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text">

</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_C460.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1772" data-file-width="2349" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_C460.jpg/220px-Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_C460.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_C460.jpg/330px-Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_C460.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_C460.jpg/440px-Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_C460.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_C460.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mk I in France, November 1939, showing original fabric-covered outer wing and two-bladed propeller</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_in_planform_arp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1711" data-file-width="1523" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Hurricane_in_planform_arp.jpg/220px-Hurricane_in_planform_arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Hurricane_in_planform_arp.jpg/330px-Hurricane_in_planform_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Hurricane_in_planform_arp.jpg/440px-Hurricane_in_planform_arp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_in_planform_arp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Underside view of <i>R4118</i>, a preserved Hurricane from the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hawker Hurricane is a low-wing <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilever</a> <a href="/wiki/Monoplane" title="Monoplane">monoplane</a> outfitted with retractable undercarriage and an enclosed cockpit for the pilot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_468_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_468-27">[27]</a></sup> A clean, single-seat fighter, it was developed to provide a competent combatant for aerial combat against the latest fighter designs that were emerging amongst the air services of other powers of the era. The Hurricane was initially armed with an arrangement of eight remotely-operated wing-mounted Browning machine guns, intended for conducting rapid engagements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_468_473_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_468_473-28">[28]</a></sup> The Hurricane was typically equipped for flying under both day and night conditions, being provided with navigation lights, Harley <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landing_light" title="Landing light">landing lights</a>, complete blind-flying equipment, and two-way <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radios</a>. Upon its entry to service, much of the performance data was intentionally concealed from the general public, but it was known that the type possessed a speed range of 6:1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_473_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_473-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though faster and more advanced than the RAF's current front line biplane fighters, the design of the Hurricane's construction was already considered to be somewhat outdated when introduced to service and resembled those used on the earlier biplanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_467_470_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_467_470-30">[30]</a></sup> Hawker had decided to employ its traditional construction techniques instead of radical measures such as the adoption of a stressed-skin metal exterior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_467_468_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_467_468-31">[31]</a></sup> The primary structure comprised a <a href="/wiki/Warren_truss" title="Warren truss">Warren truss</a> box-girder that made use of high-tensile steel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longerons" title="Longerons">longerons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duralumin" title="Duralumin">duralumin</a> cross-bracing, which were mechanically fastened instead of <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welded</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_469_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_469-32">[32]</a></sup> Over this, a secondary structured composed of wooden <a href="/wiki/Former" title="Former">formers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stringer_(aircraft)" title="Stringer (aircraft)">stringers</a> gave the fuselage a rounded external shape, which carried a <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_dope" title="Aircraft dope">doped</a> <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a> covering. The majority of the external surfaces were linen, save for a section between the cockpit and the engine cowling that used lightweight metal panels instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_469_470_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_469_470-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similarly, a simple steel tube structure in the nose of the fuselage was used to support the engine; detachable panels across the cowling provided access to most of the engine's areas for inspection or adjustment purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_472_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_472-34">[34]</a></sup> Installed underneath the fuselage, the liquid-cooled radiator has a rectangular opening to its aft; this is covered by a hinged flap via which the pilot was able to actively vary the cooling level. An atypical feature for the era was the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tungum" title="Tungum">tungum</a> pipes throughout the cooling system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_472_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_472-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially, the structure of the Hurricane's <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilever</a> wing consisted of two steel spars, which possessed considerable strength and stiffness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_469_471_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_469_471-35">[35]</a></sup> The wing was described by aviation publication <a href="/wiki/Flight_International" title="Flight International">Flight</a> as being relatively straightforward to manufacture, employing simple vertical <a href="/wiki/Jig_(tool)" title="Jig (tool)">jigs</a> to attach the two spars, after which the wing ribs would be installed using horizontal bolts, forming separate units between the front and rear spars. <a href="/wiki/Hydraulics" title="Hydraulics">Hydraulically</a>-actuated split <a href="/wiki/Trailing_edge" title="Trailing edge">trailing edge</a> <a href="/wiki/Flap_(aeronautics)" title="Flap (aeronautics)">flaps</a> were present on the inner end of the wings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_471_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_471-36">[36]</a></sup> The original wing was predominantly fabric-covered, like the fuselage, while some use of lightweight metal sheets was made upon the exterior surface of the inner wing and its <a href="/wiki/Leading_edge" title="Leading edge">leading edge</a>. The majority of the <a href="/wiki/Flight_control_surfaces" title="Flight control surfaces">Flight control surfaces</a>, such as the Frise-type <a href="/wiki/Aileron" title="Aileron">ailerons</a>, also had fabric coverings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_471_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_471-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>An all-metal, stressed-skin wing of <a href="/wiki/Duralumin" title="Duralumin">duraluminium</a> (a DERD specification similar to AA2024) was introduced in April 1939 and was used for all of the later marks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greatac_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-greatac-12">[12]</a></sup> "The metal skinned wings allowed a diving speed that was 80 mph (130 km/h) higher than the fabric-covered ones. They were very different in construction but were interchangeable with the fabric-covered wings; one trials Hurricane, <i>L1877</i>, was even flown with a fabric-covered port wing and metal-covered starboard wing. The great advantage of the metal-covered wings over the fabric ones was that the metal ones could carry far greater stress loads without needing so much structure beneath."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._12_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._12-37">[37]</a></sup> Several fabric-wing Hurricanes were still in service during the Battle of Britain, although a good number had had their wings replaced during servicing or after repair. Changing the wings only required three hours work per aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._12_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._12-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hurricane was furnished with a laterally-retracting <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">undercarriage</a>, the main undercarriage units being able to slide into recesses within the wing upon retraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_472_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_472-34">[34]</a></sup> Hinged telescopic Vickers-built legs are attached to the bottom boom of the wing's forward spar. A hydraulic jack served to actuate the undercarriage, folding and pivoting the legs as to reposition the wheel unit rearwards well as inwards in order to clear the front spar when retracted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_472_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_472-34">[34]</a></sup> Two separate hydraulic systems, one being power-operated and the other hand operated, are present for the deployment and retraction of the undercarriage; in the event of both failing, pilots can release the retaining catches holding the undercarriage in place, deploying the wheels to the 'down' position using weight alone. A wide wheel-track was used to allow for considerable stability during ground movements and to enable tight turns to be performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_471_472_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_471_472-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The prototype and early production Hurricanes were fitted with a Watts two-bladed fixed-pitch wooden propeller. <a href="/wiki/Flight_International" title="Flight International"><i>Flight</i></a> commented of this arrangement: "Many have expressed surprise that the Hurricane is not fitted with variable-pitch airscrews".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fli_38_473_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-fli_38_473-29">[29]</a></sup> The original two-bladed propeller was found to be inefficient at low airspeeds and the aircraft required a long ground run to get airborne, which caused concern at Fighter Command. Accordingly, trials with a <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland" title="De Havilland">De Havilland</a> <a href="/wiki/Variable-pitch_propeller" title="Variable-pitch propeller">variable-pitch propeller</a> demonstrated a reduction in the Hurricane's take-off run from 1,230 to 750 ft (370 to 230 m). Deliveries of these began in April 1939: this was later replaced by the hydraulically operated constant-speed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotol" title="Rotol">Rotol</a> propeller, which came into service in time for the Battle of Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:26%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>Then, with tail trimmer set, throttle and mixture lever fully forward... and puffs of grey exhaust smoke soon clearing at maximum r.p.m. came the surprise! There was no sudden surge of acceleration, but with a thunderous roar from the exhausts just ahead on either side of the windscreen, only a steady increase in speed... In retrospect that first Hurricane sortie was a moment of elation, but also of relief. Apart from the new scale of speeds that the pilot had to adapt to, the Hurricane had all the qualities of its stable, secure biplane predecessor the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hart" title="Hawker Hart">Hart</a>, but enhanced by livelier controls, greater precision and all this performance.</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><small><a href="/wiki/Roland_Beamont" title="Roland Beamont">Roland Beamont</a>, a trainee pilot, describing his first flight in a Hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></small></cite></div>
<p>Camm's priority was to provide the pilot with good all-round visibility. To this end, the cockpit was mounted reasonably high in the fuselage, creating a distinctive "hump-backed" silhouette. Pilot access to the cockpit was aided by a retractable "<a href="/wiki/Stirrup" title="Stirrup">stirrup</a>" mounted below the trailing edge of the port wing. This was linked to a spring-loaded hinged flap which covered a handhold on the fuselage, just behind the cockpit. When the flap was shut, the footstep retracted into the fuselage. In addition, both <a href="/wiki/Wing_root" title="Wing root">wing roots</a> were coated with strips of non-slip material.</p>
<p>An advantage of the steel-tube structure was that cannon shells could pass right through the wood and fabric covering without exploding. Even if one of the steel tubes were damaged, the repair work required was relatively simple and could be done by ground crew at the airfield. Damage to a <a href="/wiki/Stressed_skin" title="Stressed skin">stressed skin</a> structure, as used by the Spitfire, required more specialised equipment to repair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The old-fashioned structure also permitted the assembly of Hurricanes with relatively basic equipment under field conditions. Crated Hurricanes were assembled at <a href="/wiki/Sekondi-Takoradi" title="Sekondi-Takoradi">Takoradi</a> in West Africa and flown across the Sahara to the Middle East theatre and, to save space, some <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> aircraft carriers carried their reserve Sea Hurricanes dismantled into their major assemblies, which were slung up on the hangar bulkheads and deckhead for reassembly when needed.</p>
<p>In contrast, the contemporary Spitfire used all-metal <a href="/wiki/Monocoque" title="Monocoque">monocoque</a> construction and was thus both lighter and stronger, though less tolerant to bullet damage. With its ease of maintenance, widely set landing gear and benign flying characteristics, the Hurricane remained in use in theatres of operations where reliability, easy handling and a stable gun platform were more important than performance, typically in roles like ground attack. One of the design requirements of the original specification was that both the Hurricane and the Spitfire were also to be used as <a href="/wiki/Night_fighter" title="Night fighter">night fighters</a>. The Hurricane proved to be a relatively simple aircraft to fly at night, and was instrumental in shooting down several German aircraft on night raids. From early 1941 the Hurricane was also used as an "intruder" aircraft, patrolling German airfields in France at night to catch bombers taking off or landing.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operational_history">Operational history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Operational history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-war">Pre-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pre-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_IV_RP_-_prepared_for_presentation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="907" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_IV_RP_-_prepared_for_presentation.jpg/220px-Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_IV_RP_-_prepared_for_presentation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_IV_RP_-_prepared_for_presentation.jpg/330px-Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_IV_RP_-_prepared_for_presentation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_IV_RP_-_prepared_for_presentation.jpg/440px-Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_IV_RP_-_prepared_for_presentation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_IV_RP_-_prepared_for_presentation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Yugoslavian Hurricane Mark. I</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the middle of 1938, the first 50 Hurricanes had reached squadrons and, at that time, it had been assessed that the rate of production was slightly greater than the RAF's capacity to introduce the new aircraft, which had already been accelerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_6_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_6-42">[42]</a></sup> Accordingly, the British government gave Hawker the clearance to sell excess aircraft to nations that were likely to oppose German expansion. As a result, there were some modest export sales made to other countries; at the earliest opportunity, a former RAF Hurricane I was dispatched to <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a> for evaluation purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_6_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-mason_6-42">[42]</a></sup> Shortly after this evaluation, an order for 24 Hurricane Mark Is for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Yugoslav_Air_Force" title="Royal Yugoslav Air Force">Royal Yugoslav Air Force</a> was received; this was followed by the purchase of a <a href="/wiki/Licensed_production" title="Licensed production">production licence</a> for the Hurricane by Yugoslavia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_6_7_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-mason_6_7-25">[25]</a></sup> Yugoslavia Hurricanes saw action against the <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Yugoslavia" title="Invasion of Yugoslavia">invasion of Yugoslavia</a> in 1941 by the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_7_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-mason_7-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hurricane production was increased as part of a plan to create a reserve of <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">attrition</a> aircraft as well as re-equip existing squadrons and newly formed ones such as those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auxiliary_Air_Force" title="Auxiliary Air Force">Auxiliary Air Force</a>. Expansion scheme E included a target of 500 fighters of all types by the start of 1938. By the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Munich_Crisis" title="Munich Crisis">Munich Crisis</a>, there were only two fully operational RAF squadrons of the planned 12 to be equipped with Hurricanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> By the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_invasion_of_Poland" title="German invasion of Poland">German invasion of Poland</a> there were 18 operational Hurricane squadrons, and three more that were in the process of converting.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Phoney_War">The Phoney War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The Phoney War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_-_France_1939-1940_C1512.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="610" data-file-width="800" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hawker_Hurricane_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_-_France_1939-1940_C1512.jpg/220px-Hawker_Hurricane_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_-_France_1939-1940_C1512.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hawker_Hurricane_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_-_France_1939-1940_C1512.jpg/330px-Hawker_Hurricane_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_-_France_1939-1940_C1512.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hawker_Hurricane_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_-_France_1939-1940_C1512.jpg/440px-Hawker_Hurricane_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_-_France_1939-1940_C1512.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_-_France_1939-1940_C1512.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Personnel of <a href="/wiki/No._85_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 85 Squadron RAF">85 Sqn</a> next to a Hurricane I, <a href="/wiki/Lille" title="Lille">Lille</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nord_(French_department)" title="Nord (French department)">Seclin</a>, France, on 10 May 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hurricane had its first combat action on 21 October 1939, at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Phoney_War" title="Phoney War">Phoney War</a>. That day, “A” Flight of <a href="/wiki/No._46_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 46 Squadron RAF">46 Squadron</a> took off from North Coates satellite airfield, on the <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">Lincolnshire</a> coast, and was directed to intercept a formation of nine <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_115" title="Heinkel He 115">Heinkel He 115</a>B floatplanes from 1/KüFlGr 906, searching for ships to attack in the North Sea. The Heinkels had already been attacked and damaged by two Spitfires from <a href="/wiki/No._72_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 72 Squadron RAF">72 Squadron</a> when six Hurricanes intercepted them, which were flying at sea level in an attempt to avoid fighter attacks. The Hurricanes shot down four of the enemy in rapid succession, 46 Squadron claiming five and the Spitfire pilots two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._12._44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._12.-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to a request from the French government for the provision of 10 fighter squadrons to provide air support, Air Chief Marshal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugh_Dowding,_1st_Baron_Dowding" title="Hugh Dowding, 1st Baron Dowding">Sir Hugh Dowding</a>, Commander-in-Chief of RAF Fighter Command, insisted that this number would deplete British defences severely, and so initially only four squadrons of Hurricanes, <a href="/wiki/No._1_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 1 Squadron RAF">1</a>, <a href="/wiki/No._73_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 73 Squadron RAF">73</a>, <a href="/wiki/No._85_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 85 Squadron RAF">85</a> and <a href="/wiki/No._87_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 87 Squadron RAF">87</a>, were relocated to France, keeping Spitfires back for "Home" defence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_8_9_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_8_9-45">[45]</a></sup> The first to arrive was No.73 Squadron on 10 September 1939, followed shortly by the other three. A little later, <a href="/wiki/No._607_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 607 Squadron RAF">607</a> and <a href="/wiki/No._615_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 615 Squadron RAF">615 Squadrons</a> joined them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_9_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_9-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>After his first flight in October 1939, Hurricane pilot <a href="/wiki/Roland_Beamont" title="Roland Beamont">Roland Beamont</a> subsequently flew operationally with 87 Squadron, claiming three enemy aircraft during the French campaign, and delivered great praise for his aircraft's performance:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Throughout the bad days of 1940, 87 Sqn had maintained a proficient formation aerobatic team, the precise flying controls and responsive engines permitting precision formation through loops, barrel rolls, 1g semi-stall turns and rolls off half-loops ... My Hurricane was never hit in the Battles of France and Britain, and in over 700 hr on type I never experienced an engine failure.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Roland Beamont, summarising his wartime experience as a pilot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_I_46_Squadron.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="827" data-file-width="1707" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Hurricane_I_46_Squadron.jpg/220px-Hurricane_I_46_Squadron.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Hurricane_I_46_Squadron.jpg/330px-Hurricane_I_46_Squadron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Hurricane_I_46_Squadron.jpg/440px-Hurricane_I_46_Squadron.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_I_46_Squadron.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane Mk I of the <a href="/wiki/No._46_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 46 Squadron RAF">46th Squadron RAF</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Campaign" title="Norwegian Campaign">Norwegian campaign</a>, May 1940. This aircraft was abandoned in Norway</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the opening months of the war were characterised by little air activity in general, there were sporadic engagements and aerial skirmishes between the two sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_9_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-mason_9-46">[46]</a></sup> On 30 October 1939 Hurricanes saw action over France. That day, Pilot Officer <a href="/wiki/Boy_Mould" title="Boy Mould">P. W. O. "Boy" Mould</a> of 1 Squadron, flying Hurricane L1842, shot down a <a href="/wiki/Dornier_Do_17" title="Dornier Do 17">Dornier Do 17</a>P from 2(F)/123. The German aircraft, sent to photograph Allied airfields close to the border, fell in flames about 10 miles (16 km) west of <a href="/wiki/Toul" title="Toul">Toul</a>. Mould was the first RAF pilot to down an enemy aircraft on the European continent in the Second World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_pp._15.E2.80.9316._48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_pp._15.E2.80.9316.-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[N 1]</a></sup> According to Mason, the experiences gained in these early engagements proved invaluable in developing tactics which became tried and tested, and rapidly spread throughout Fighter Command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_9_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-mason_9-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 6 November 1939 Pilot Officer <a href="/wiki/Peter_Ayerst" title="Peter Ayerst">P.V. Ayerst</a> from 73 Squadron was the first to clash with a <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109">Messerschmitt Bf 109</a>. After the dogfight, he came back with five holes in his fuselage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._18._50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._18.-50">[49]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flying_Officer" title="Flying Officer">Flying Officer</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cobber_Kain" title="Cobber Kain">E. J. "Cobber" Kain</a>, a New Zealander, was responsible for 73 Squadron's first victory on 8 November 1939 while stationed at <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tain-Rouvres_Air_Base" title="Étain-Rouvres Air Base">Rouvres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> He went on to become one of the RAF's first <a href="/wiki/Flying_ace" title="Flying ace">fighter aces</a> of the war, being credited with 16 kills. On 22 December the Hurricanes in France suffered their first losses: three of them, while trying to intercept an unidentified aircraft between <a href="/wiki/Metz" title="Metz">Metz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thionville" title="Thionville">Thionville</a>, were jumped by four Bf 109Es from III./JG 53, with their <i><a href="/wiki/Gruppenkommandeur" title="Gruppenkommandeur">Gruppenkommandeur</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> ace Captain <a href="/wiki/Werner_M%C3%B6lders" title="Werner Mölders">Werner Mölders</a>, in the lead. Mölders and <i>Leutnant</i> <a href="/wiki/Hans_von_Hahn" title="Hans von Hahn">Hans von Hahn</a> shot down the Hurricanes of Sergeant R. M. Perry and J. Winn for no loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._18._50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._18.-50">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_France">Battle of France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Battle of France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C_001234Hurricane1Sqn1940.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="192" data-file-width="282" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/C_001234Hurricane1Sqn1940.jpg/220px-C_001234Hurricane1Sqn1940.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/10/C_001234Hurricane1Sqn1940.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C_001234Hurricane1Sqn1940.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane I of <a href="/wiki/No._1_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 1 Squadron RAF">1 Sqn</a> being refuelled at <a href="/wiki/Vassincourt" title="Vassincourt">Vassincourt</a>, France</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In May 1940, Nos. 3, 79 and 504 Squadrons reinforced the earlier units as Germany's <i><a href="/wiki/Blitzkrieg" title="Blitzkrieg">Blitzkrieg</a></i> gathered momentum. On 10 May, the first day of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a>, Flight Lieutenant R. E. Lovett and Flying Officer "Fanny" Orton, of 73 Squadron, were the first R.A.F pilots to engage enemy aircraft in the campaign. They attacked one of three <a href="/wiki/Dornier_Do_17" title="Dornier Do 17">Dornier Do 17s</a> from <i>4. Staffel/<a href="/wiki/Kampfgeschwader_2" title="Kampfgeschwader 2">KG 2</a></i> that were flying over their airfield at <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tain-Rouvres_Air_Base" title="Étain-Rouvres Air Base">Rouvres-en-Woevre</a>. The Dornier went away unscathed, while Orton was hit by defensive fire and had to force land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._24._52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._24.-52">[51]</a></sup> On the same day the Hurricane squadrons claimed 42 German aircraft, none of them fighters, shot down during 208 sorties; seven Hurricanes were lost but no pilots were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._24._52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._24.-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 12 May several Hurricanes units were committed to escort bombers. That morning, five <a href="/wiki/Fairey_Battle" title="Fairey Battle">Fairey Battle</a> volunteer crews from 12 Squadron took off from <a href="/wiki/Amifontaine" title="Amifontaine">Amifontaine</a> base to bomb Vroenhoven and Veldwezelt bridges on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meuse_(river)" title="Meuse (river)">Meuse</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Maastricht" title="Maastricht">Maastricht</a>. The escort consisted of eight Hurricanes of 1 Squadron, with Squadron Leader P. J. H. "Bull" Halahan in the lead. When the formation approached Maastricht, it was bounced by 16 Bf 109Es from <i>2./JG 27</i>. Two Battles and two Hurricanes (including Halahan's) were shot down, two more Battles were brought down by flak and the fifth bomber had to crash-land. The 1 Squadron pilots claimed four Messerschmitts and two <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_112" title="Heinkel He 112">Heinkel He 112s</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[N 2]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a> actually lost only one Bf 109.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_pp._41-42._54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_pp._41-42.-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-group_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-group-55">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 13 May 1940, a further 32 Hurricanes arrived. All ten requested Hurricane squadrons were then operating from French soil and felt the full force of the Nazi offensive. The following day, Hurricanes suffered heavy losses: 27 being shot down, 22 by Messerschmitts, with 15 pilots killed (another died some days later), including Squadron Leader J. B. Parnall (504 Sqn),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[N 3]</a></sup> and the Australian ace <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flying_Officer" title="Flying Officer">Flying Officer</a> <a href="/wiki/Les_Clisby" title="Les Clisby">Les Clisby</a> (1 Sqn).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._47._57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._47.-57">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[N 4]</a></sup> On the same day, 3 Squadron claimed 17 German aircraft shot down, 85 and 87 Squadrons together claimed four victories, while 607 Squadron claimed nine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_pp._48-49._59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_pp._48-49.-59">[55]</a></sup> During the following three days (15–17 May), no fewer than 51 Hurricanes were lost, in combat or in accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._49._60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._49.-60">[56]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_-_Reims-Champagne_-_Royal_Air_Force_France,_1939-1940._C1731.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="800" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Hawker_Hurricane_-_Reims-Champagne_-_Royal_Air_Force_France%2C_1939-1940._C1731.jpg/220px-Hawker_Hurricane_-_Reims-Champagne_-_Royal_Air_Force_France%2C_1939-1940._C1731.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Hawker_Hurricane_-_Reims-Champagne_-_Royal_Air_Force_France%2C_1939-1940._C1731.jpg/330px-Hawker_Hurricane_-_Reims-Champagne_-_Royal_Air_Force_France%2C_1939-1940._C1731.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Hawker_Hurricane_-_Reims-Champagne_-_Royal_Air_Force_France%2C_1939-1940._C1731.jpg/440px-Hawker_Hurricane_-_Reims-Champagne_-_Royal_Air_Force_France%2C_1939-1940._C1731.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_-_Reims-Champagne_-_Royal_Air_Force_France,_1939-1940._C1731.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mechanics servicing the engine of a Hurricane I of <a href="/wiki/No._501_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 501 Squadron RAF">501 Sqn</a> at No. 1 Repair Centre, <a href="/wiki/Reims" title="Reims">Reims</a>, <a href="/wiki/Champagne_(wine_region)" title="Champagne (wine region)">Champagne</a>, France</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 17 May, the end of the first week of fighting, only three of the squadrons were near operational strength, but the Hurricanes had managed to destroy nearly twice as many German aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup> On 18 May 1940, air combat continued from dawn to dusk; Hurricane pilots claimed 57 German aircraft and 20 probables (Luftwaffe records show 39 aircraft lost). The following day, 1 and 73 Squadrons claimed 11 German aircraft (three by "Cobber" Kain and three by Paul Richey). On these two days Hurricanes suffered heavier losses, with 68 Hurricanes shot down or forced to crash-land due to combat damage. Fifteen pilots were killed, eight were taken prisoner and 11 injured. Two-thirds of the Hurricanes had been shot down by Messerschmitt Bf 109s and <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_110" title="Messerschmitt Bf 110">Bf 110s</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_pp._51._62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_pp._51.-62">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the afternoon of 20 May 1940, the Hurricane units based in northern France were ordered to abandon their bases on the continent and return to Great Britain. On the same day, "Bull" Halahan requested the repatriation of the pilots serving in 1 Squadron. During the previous 10 days, the unit had been the most successful of the campaign; it had claimed 63 victories for the loss of five pilots: two killed, one taken prisoner and two hospitalised. 1 Squadron was awarded ten DFCs and three DFMs during the <i>Blitzkrieg</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._52._63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._52.-63">[59]</a></sup> On the evening of 21 May, the only Hurricanes still operational were those of the <a href="/wiki/RAF_Advanced_Air_Striking_Force" title="RAF Advanced Air Striking Force">AASF</a> that had been moved to bases around <a href="/wiki/Troyes" title="Troyes">Troyes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._55._64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._55.-64">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 11 days of fighting in France and over Dunkirk from 10–21 May, Hurricane pilots claimed 499 kills and 123 probables. Contemporary German records, examined postwar, attribute 299 Luftwaffe aircraft destroyed and 65 seriously damaged by RAF fighters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> The last 66 Hurricanes of the 452 engaged during the Battle of France left France on 21 June; 178 were abandoned at several airfields, notably <a href="/wiki/Merville,_Nord" title="Merville, Nord">Merville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abbeville" title="Abbeville">Abbeville</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lille" title="Lille">Lille</a>/<a href="/wiki/Seclin" title="Seclin">Seclin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._55._64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._55.-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p._23._66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p._23.-66">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operation_Dynamo">Operation Dynamo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Operation Dynamo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk_evacuation" title="Dunkirk evacuation">Operation Dynamo</a> (the evacuation from Dunkirk of British, French and Belgian troops cut off by the German army during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dunkirk" title="Battle of Dunkirk">Battle of Dunkirk</a>), the Hawker Hurricanes operated from British bases. Between 26 May and 3 June 1940, the 14 Hurricane units involved were credited with 108 air victories. A total of 27 Hurricane pilots became aces during Operation Dynamo, led by Canadian Pilot Officer W. L. <a href="/wiki/Willie_McKnight" title="Willie McKnight">Willie McKnight</a> (10 victories) and Pilot Officer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percival_Stanley_Turner" title="Percival Stanley Turner">Percival Stanley Turner</a> (seven victories), who served in No. 242 Squadron, consisting mostly of Canadian personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p.58_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p.58-67">[63]</a></sup> Losses were 22 pilots killed and three captured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_p.57_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes_p.57-68">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>Over Dunkirk, the Luftwaffe suffered its first serious rebuff of the war. As Galland has noted, the nature and style of the air battles over the beaches should have provided a warning as to the inherent weaknesses of the Luftwaffe's force structure. Admittedly, the Germans fought at a disadvantage. Although positioned forward at captured airfields, the Bf 109 was at the outer limits of its range and possessed less flying time over Dunkirk than did the "Hurricanes" and "Spitfires" operating from southern England. German bombers were still located in western Germany and had even farther to fly. Thus, the Luftwaffe could not bring its full weight to bear so that when its bombers hammered those on the beaches or embarking, the RAF intervened in a significant fashion. German aircraft losses were high, and British fighter attacks often prevented German bombers from performing with full effectiveness. Both sides suffered heavy losses. During the nine days from May 26 through June 3, the RAF lost 177 aircraft destroyed or damaged; the Germans lost 240. For much of the Luftwaffe, Dunkirk came as a nasty shock. Fliegerkorps II reported in its war diary that it lost more aircraft on the 27th attacking the evacuation than it had lost in the previous ten days of the campaign.</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><small>Murray. <i>Strategy for Defeat. The Luftwaffe 1935–1945</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup></small></cite></div>
<p>On 27 May 1940, in one of the final mass encounters of the <i>Blitzkrieg</i>, 13 Hurricanes from 501 Squadron intercepted 24 <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_111" title="Heinkel He 111">Heinkel He 111s</a> escorted by 20 Bf 110s; during the ensuing battle, 11 Heinkels were claimed as "kills" and others damaged, with little damage to the Hurricanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup> On 7 June 1940, "Cobber" Kain, the first RAF ace of the war, got word that he was to return to England for "rest leave" at an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operational_Training_Unit" title="Operational Training Unit">Operational Training Unit</a>. On leaving his airfield, he put on an impromptu aerobatic display and was killed when his Hurricane crashed after completing a <a href="/wiki/Aerobatic_maneuver" title="Aerobatic maneuver">loop</a> and attempting some low altitude <a href="/wiki/Aerobatic_maneuver" title="Aerobatic maneuver">"flick" rolls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initial engagements with the Luftwaffe had showed the Hurricane to be a tight-turning and steady platform, but the Watts two-bladed propeller was clearly unsuitable. At least one pilot complained of how a Heinkel 111 was able to pull away from him in a chase, yet by this time the Heinkel was obsolete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._12_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._12-37">[37]</a></sup> At the start of the war, the engine ran on standard 87 <a href="/wiki/Octane" title="Octane">octane</a> <a href="/wiki/Avgas" title="Avgas">aviation spirit</a>. From early 1940, increasing quantities of 100 octane fuel imported from the U.S. became available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup> In February 1940, Hurricanes with the Merlin II and III engines began to receive modifications to allow for an additional 6 psi (41 kPa) of <a href="/wiki/Supercharger" title="Supercharger">supercharger</a> boost for five minutes (although there are accounts of its use for 30 minutes continuously).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gleed_1942.2C_p._61._74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gleed_1942.2C_p._61.-74">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The extra supercharger boost, which increased engine output by nearly 250 hp (190 kW), gave the Hurricane an approximate increase in speed of 25 to 35 mph (40 to 56 km/h), under 15,000 ft (4,600 m)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gleed_1942.2C_p._61._74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gleed_1942.2C_p._61.-74">[70]</a></sup> altitude and greatly increased the aircraft's climb rate. "Overboost" or "pulling the plug", a form of <a href="/wiki/War_emergency_power" title="War emergency power">war emergency power</a> as it was called in later Second World War aircraft, was an important wartime modification that allowed the Hurricane to be more competitive against the <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109">Bf 109E</a> and to increase its margin of superiority over the <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_110" title="Messerschmitt Bf 110">Bf 110C</a>, especially at low altitude. With the +12 psi (83 kPa) "emergency boost", the Merlin III was able to generate 1,310 hp (980 kW) at 9,000 ft (2,700 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_Lieutenant" title="Flight Lieutenant">Flight Lieutenant</a> Ian Gleed of 87 Squadron wrote about the effect of using the extra boost on the Hurricane while chasing a Bf 109 at low altitude on 19 May 1940: "Damn! We're flat out as it is. Here goes with the tit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[N 5]</a></sup> A jerk – boost's shot up to 12 pounds; speed's increased by 30 mph. I'm gaining ground – 700, 600, 500 yards. Give him a burst. No, hold your fire you fool! He hasn't seen you yet..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gleed_1942.2C_p._61._74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gleed_1942.2C_p._61.-74">[70]</a></sup> Gleed ran out of ammunition before he could shoot the 109 down although he left it heavily damaged and flying at about 50 ft (15 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[N 6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hurricanes equipped with <a href="/wiki/Dowty_Rotol" title="Dowty Rotol">Rotol</a> constant-speed propellers were delivered to RAF squadrons in May 1940, with deliveries continuing throughout the Battle of Britain. According to aviation author David Donald, the Rotol propeller had the effect of transforming the Hurricane's performance from "disappointing" to "acceptable mediocrity"; modified aircraft were reportedly much sought after among squadrons which had also been equipped with Hurricanes that were fitted with the older de Havilland two-position propeller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_5_6_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_5_6-79">[73]</a></sup></p>
