<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Britain">Battle of Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Battle of Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_I_1_Sqn_RAF_at_RAF_Wittering_1940.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="596" data-file-width="800" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Hurricane_I_1_Sqn_RAF_at_RAF_Wittering_1940.jpg/220px-Hurricane_I_1_Sqn_RAF_at_RAF_Wittering_1940.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Hurricane_I_1_Sqn_RAF_at_RAF_Wittering_1940.jpg/330px-Hurricane_I_1_Sqn_RAF_at_RAF_Wittering_1940.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Hurricane_I_1_Sqn_RAF_at_RAF_Wittering_1940.jpg/440px-Hurricane_I_1_Sqn_RAF_at_RAF_Wittering_1940.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_I_1_Sqn_RAF_at_RAF_Wittering_1940.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane I of <a href="/wiki/No._1_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 1 Squadron RAF">1 Sqn</a> flown by Plt Off A.V. Clowes.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the end of June 1940, following the fall of France, the majority of the RAF's 36 fighter squadrons were equipped with Hurricanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_12_13_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_12_13-80">[74]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a> officially lasted from 10 July until 31 October 1940, but the heaviest fighting took place between 8 August and 21 September. Both the Supermarine Spitfire and the Hurricane are renowned for their part in defending Britain against the Luftwaffe; generally, the Spitfire would intercept the German fighters, leaving Hurricanes to concentrate on the bombers, but, despite the undoubted abilities of the "thoroughbred" Spitfire, it was the "workhorse" Hurricane that scored the higher number of RAF victories during this period, accounting for 55 percent of the 2,739 German losses, according to Fighter Command, compared with 42 per cent by Spitfires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forgotten_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-forgotten-81">[75]</a></sup> On 8 August 1940, Hurricanes of <a href="/wiki/No._145_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 145 Squadron RAF">No. 145 Squadron</a> were recorded as having fired the first shots of the Battle of Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_12_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_12-82">[76]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:26%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>Another thing we did was to devise a manoeuvre which was aimed at getting us out of a difficult corner if we ever got into one. This may sound very extraordinary, probably, to practising pilots today, but it consisted of putting everything into the left hand front corner of the cockpit. If you saw a 109 on your tail, and it hadn't shot you down at that point, you put on full throttle, fine pitch, full left rudder, full left stick and full forward stick. This resulted in a horrible manoeuvre which was, in fact, a negative <i>g</i> spiral dive. But you would come out of the bottom with no 109 on your tail and your aeroplane intact.</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roland_Beaumont" title="Roland Beaumont">Roland Beaumont</a> describing how a Hurricane can get away from an Me109.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[77]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>As a fighter, the Hurricane had some drawbacks. It was slightly slower than both the Spitfire I and II and the Messerschmitt Bf 109E, and the thicker wing profiles compromised acceleration; but it could out-turn both of them. In spite of its performance deficiencies against the Bf 109, the Hurricane was still capable of destroying the German fighter, especially at lower altitudes. The standard tactic of the 109s was to attempt to climb higher than the RAF fighters and "bounce" them in a dive; the Hurricanes could evade such tactics by turning into the attack or going into a "corkscrew dive", which the 109s, with their lower rate of roll, found hard to counter. If a 109 was caught in a dogfight, the Hurricane was just as capable of out-turning the 109 as the Spitfire. In a stern chase, the 109 could evade the Hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 1940, the more powerful Mk IIa series 1 Hurricanes started entering service, although only in small numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[79]</a></sup> This version was capable of a maximum speed of 342 mph (550 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hurricane was a steady gun platform,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[81]</a></sup> and had demonstrated its ruggedness as several were badly damaged yet returned to base. But the Hurricane's construction made it dangerous if it caught fire; the wood frames and fabric covering of the rear fuselage allowed fire to spread through the rear fuselage structure easily. In addition, the gravity fuel tank in the forward fuselage sat right in front of the instrument panel, without any form of protection for the pilot. Many Hurricane pilots were seriously burned as a consequence of a jet of flame which could burn through the instrument panel. This became of such concern to <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dowding" title="Hugh Dowding">Hugh Dowding</a> that he had Hawker retrofit the fuselage tanks of the Hurricanes with a self-expanding rubber coating called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Linatex&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Linatex (page does not exist)">Linatex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup> If the tank happened to be punctured by a bullet, the linatex coating would expand when soaked with petrol and seal it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup> Some Hurricane pilots also felt that the fuel tanks in the wings, although they were protected with a layer of Linatex, were vulnerable from behind, and it was thought that these, not the fuselage tank, were the main fire risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_of_Britain_HU57450.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="618" data-file-width="800" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/The_Battle_of_Britain_HU57450.jpg/220px-The_Battle_of_Britain_HU57450.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/The_Battle_of_Britain_HU57450.jpg/330px-The_Battle_of_Britain_HU57450.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/The_Battle_of_Britain_HU57450.jpg/440px-The_Battle_of_Britain_HU57450.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_of_Britain_HU57450.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Groundcrew refuelling a Hurricane Mk I of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/No._32_(The_Royal)_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 32 (The Royal) Squadron RAF">32 Sqn</a>, <a href="/wiki/London_Biggin_Hill_Airport" title="London Biggin Hill Airport">RAF Biggin Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Bromley" title="London Borough of Bromley">Bromley</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, August 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 10 July to 11 August 1940, RAF fighters fired at 114 German bombers and shot down 80, a destruction ratio of 70 per cent. Against the Bf 109, the RAF fighters attacked 70 and shot down 54 of these, a ratio of 77 per cent. It has been suggested that part of the success of the British fighters was possibly due to the use of the <a href="/wiki/Incendiary_ammunition#World_War_II" title="Incendiary ammunition">de Wilde incendiary round</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hurricane with the highest number of kills during the Battle of Britain was P3308, a Mk1, flown between 15 August and 7 October 1940 by RAF (auxiliary) pilot <a href="/wiki/Archie_McKellar" title="Archie McKellar">Archie McKellar</a> of 605 Squadron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup> He is credited with 21 kills, 19 of those in a Hurricane during the Battle of Britain. On 7 October he is credited with shooting down 5 Bf 109s, making him one of only 2 RAF pilots (the other <a href="/wiki/Brian_Carbury" title="Brian Carbury">Brian Carbury</a> of New Zealand) to become an Ace in a Day during the Battle of Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[89]</a></sup> During his brief fighting career, McKellar earned the <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Order" title="Distinguished Service Order">DSO</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Flying_Cross_(United_Kingdom)" title="Distinguished Flying Cross (United Kingdom)">DFC</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Medal_bar" title="Medal bar">Bar</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup> MacKellar has remained in relative obscurity in Battle of Britain history, as he was killed in action one day after the date set by the War Ministry (after the War) as the official end date for the Battle of Britain. He was killed on 1 November 1940 while taking on a superior number of Bf109s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>As in the Spitfire, the Merlin engine suffered from negative-G cut-out, a problem not cured until the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Miss_Shilling%27s_orifice" title="Miss Shilling's orifice">Miss Shilling's orifice</a> in early 1941.</p>
<p>The only Battle of Britain <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross" title="Victoria Cross">Victoria Cross</a>, and the only one awarded to a member of Fighter Command during the war,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup> was awarded to Flight Lieutenant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eric_Nicolson" title="Eric Nicolson">Eric Nicolson</a> of <a href="/wiki/No._249_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 249 Squadron RAF">249 Squadron</a> as a result of an action on 16 August 1940 when his section of three Hurricanes was "bounced" from above by Bf 110 fighters. All three were hit simultaneously. Nicolson was badly wounded, and his Hurricane was damaged and engulfed in flames. While attempting to leave the cockpit, Nicolson noticed that one of the Bf 110s had overshot his aircraft. He returned to the cockpit, which by now was an inferno, engaged the enemy, and may have shot down the Bf 110.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_13_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_13-102">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[N 7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Night_fighters_and_intruders">Night fighters and intruders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Night fighters and intruders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_IIC_87_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="702" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Hurricane_IIC_87_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg/220px-Hurricane_IIC_87_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Hurricane_IIC_87_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg/330px-Hurricane_IIC_87_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Hurricane_IIC_87_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg/440px-Hurricane_IIC_87_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_IIC_87_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wartime colour photo of Hurricane IIC <i>BE500</i> flown by Sqn Ldr <a href="/wiki/Denis_Smallwood" title="Denis Smallwood">Denis Smallwood</a> of <a href="/wiki/No._87_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 87 Squadron RAF">87 Sqn</a> in the RDM2 ("Special Night") scheme and used on intruder operations 1941–1942.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the Battle of Britain the Hurricane continued to give service; through <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">the Blitz</a> of 1941 it was the principal single-seat night fighter in Fighter Command. F/Lt. Richard Stevens claimed 14 Luftwaffe bombers flying Hurricanes in 1941. In 1942 the cannon-armed Mk IIc performed further afield, as a night intruder over occupied Europe. F/Lt. <a href="/wiki/Karel_Kuttelwascher" title="Karel Kuttelwascher">Karel Kuttelwascher</a> of <a href="/wiki/No._1_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 1 Squadron RAF">1 Squadron</a> proved the top scorer, with 15 Luftwaffe bombers claimed shot down.</p>
<p>1942 also saw the manufacture of twelve Hurricane II C(NF) night fighters, equipped with pilot-operated Air Interception Mark VI radar. After a brief operational deployment with No. 245 and <a href="/wiki/No._247_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 247 Squadron RAF">No. 247 Squadron RAF</a> during which these aircraft proved too slow for operations in Europe, the aircraft were sent to India to serve with <a href="/wiki/No._176_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 176 Squadron RAF">No. 176 Squadron RAF</a> in the defence of Calcutta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[98]</a></sup> They were withdrawn from service at the end of December 1943.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Africa">North Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: North Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maintenance_of_274_Sqn_RAF_Hurricane_Gerawala_1941.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="762" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Maintenance_of_274_Sqn_RAF_Hurricane_Gerawala_1941.jpg/220px-Maintenance_of_274_Sqn_RAF_Hurricane_Gerawala_1941.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Maintenance_of_274_Sqn_RAF_Hurricane_Gerawala_1941.jpg/330px-Maintenance_of_274_Sqn_RAF_Hurricane_Gerawala_1941.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Maintenance_of_274_Sqn_RAF_Hurricane_Gerawala_1941.jpg/440px-Maintenance_of_274_Sqn_RAF_Hurricane_Gerawala_1941.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maintenance_of_274_Sqn_RAF_Hurricane_Gerawala_1941.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Maintenance work being carried out on a Hurricane of 274 Sqdn during the siege of Tobruk</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hurricane Mk II was hastily <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropicalised</a> following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(1861%E2%80%931946)" title="Kingdom of Italy (1861–1946)">Italy</a>'s entry into the war in June 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_14_15_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_14_15-107">[100]</a></sup> These aircraft were initially ferried through France and Malta by air to <a href="/wiki/No._80_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 80 Squadron RAF">80 Squadron</a> in Egypt, replacing <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Gladiator" title="Gloster Gladiator">Gladiator</a> biplanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_15_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_15-108">[101]</a></sup> The Hurricane claimed its first kill in the Mediterranean on 19 June 1940, when F/O P.G. Wykeham-Barnes reported shooting down two <a href="/wiki/Fiat_CR.42" title="Fiat CR.42">Fiat CR.42s</a>. Hurricanes served with several British Commonwealth squadrons in the <a href="/wiki/Desert_Air_Force" title="Desert Air Force">Desert Air Force</a>. They suffered heavy losses over North Africa after the arrival of Bf 109E and F-variants and were progressively replaced in the air superiority role from June 1941 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curtiss_P-40" title="Curtiss P-40">Curtiss Tomahawks/Kittyhawks</a>. However, fighter-bomber variants ("Hurribombers") retained an edge in the ground attack role, due to their impressive armament of four 20 mm (.79 in) cannon and a 500 lb (230 kg) bomb load. From November 1941, beginning in the Libyan desert, it had to face a new formidable opponent: the new <a href="/wiki/Regia_Aeronautica" title="Regia Aeronautica">Regia Aeronautica</a> <a href="/wiki/Macchi_C.202" title="Macchi C.202">Macchi C.202</a> <i>Folgore</i>. The Italian aircraft proved superior to the Hawker fighter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_p._165._109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_p._165.-109">[102]</a></sup> and, thanks to its excellent agility and a new, <a href="/wiki/Daimler-Benz_DB_601" title="Daimler-Benz DB 601">more powerful inline engine</a> license-built by <a href="/wiki/Alfa_Romeo" title="Alfa Romeo">Alfa Romeo</a>, could outperform it in a dogfight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snedden_p._51._110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Snedden_p._51.-110">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>During and following the five-day <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_El_Alamein" title="Second Battle of El Alamein">Second Battle of El Alamein</a> artillery barrage that commenced on the night of 23 October 1942, six squadrons of Hurricanes, including the <a href="/wiki/Vickers_S" title="Vickers S">40 mm cannon</a>-armed Hurricane Mk.IID version, claimed to have destroyed 39 tanks, 212 lorries and armoured troop-carriers, 26 <a href="/wiki/Bowser_(tanker)" title="Bowser (tanker)">bowsers</a>, 42 guns, 200 various other vehicles and four small fuel and ammunition dumps, flying 842 sorties with the loss of 11 pilots. Whilst performing in a ground support role, Hurricanes based at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Castel_Benito" title="RAF Castel Benito">RAF Castel Benito</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a>, knocked out six tanks, 13 armoured vehicles, 10 lorries, five <a href="/wiki/Half-track" title="Half-track">half-tracks</a>, a gun and trailer, and a wireless van on 10 March 1943, with no losses to themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the spring of 1943, during the German <a href="/wiki/Operation_Ochsenkopf" title="Operation Ochsenkopf">Ochsenkopf offensive</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_Campaign" title="Tunisian Campaign">Tunisia</a>, Hurricane MKIIDs conducted many sorties after fog had lifted, helping to blunt the final attack at Hunts Gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defence_of_Malta">Defence of Malta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Defence of Malta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Hurricane played a significant role in the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Malta_(World_War_II)" title="Siege of Malta (World War II)">defence of Malta</a>. When Italy entered the war on 10 June 1940, Malta's air defence rested on <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Gladiator" title="Gloster Gladiator">Gloster Gladiators</a>, which managed to hold out against vastly superior numbers of the Italian air force during the following 17 days. (According to myth, after the first one was lost, the remaining three were named “Faith, Hope and Charity”; in reality, there were at least six Gladiators.) Four Hurricanes joined them at the end of June, and together they faced attacks throughout July from the 200 enemy aircraft based in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, with the loss of one Gladiator and one Hurricane. Further reinforcements arrived on 2 August in the form of 12 more Hurricanes and two <a href="/wiki/Blackburn_Skua" title="Blackburn Skua">Blackburn Skuas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[106]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[N 8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>For weeks a handful of Hurricane IIs, aided by Group Captain A.B. Woodhall's masterly controlling, had been meeting, against all the odds, the rising crescendo of <a href="/wiki/Albert_Kesselring" title="Albert Kesselring">Field Marshal Kesselring</a>'s relentless attacks on <a href="/wiki/Grand_Harbour" title="Grand Harbour">Grand Harbour</a> and the airfields. Outnumbered, usually, by 12 or 14 to one and, later – with the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109">Bf 109</a>Fs in Sicily – outperformed, the pilots of the few old aircraft which the ground crews struggled valiantly to keep serviceable, went on pressing their attacks, ploughing their way through the German fighter screens, and our flak, to close in with the <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_87" title="Junkers Ju 87">Ju 87s</a> and <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_88" title="Junkers Ju 88">88s</a> as they dived for their targets.</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><small>Wing Commander <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percy_Lucas" title="Percy Lucas">P.B. "Laddie" Lucas</a> <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Order" title="Distinguished Service Order">D.S.O.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Flying_Cross_(United_Kingdom)" title="Distinguished Flying Cross (United Kingdom)">D.F.C.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bader_147-155_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-bader_147-155-115">[107]</a></sup></small></cite></div>
<p>The increasing number of British aircraft on the island, at last, prompted the Italians to employ German <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_87" title="Junkers Ju 87">Junkers Ju 87</a> dive bombers to try to destroy the airfields. Finally, in an attempt to overcome the stiff resistance put up by these few aircraft, the Luftwaffe took up base on the Sicilian airfields, only to find that Malta was not an easy target. After numerous attacks on the island over the following months, and the arrival of an extra 23 Hurricanes at the end of April 1941, and a further delivery a month later, the Luftwaffe left Sicily for the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Russian Front</a> in June that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Malta was situated on the increasingly important sea supply route for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_African_campaign" title="North African campaign">North African campaign</a>, the Luftwaffe returned with a vengeance for a second assault on the island at the beginning of 1942. It wasn't until March, when the onslaught was at its height, that 15 Spitfires flew in off the carrier <a href="/wiki/HMS_Eagle_(1918)" title="HMS Eagle (1918)">HMS <i>Eagle</i></a> to join with the Hurricanes already stationed there and bolster the defence, but many of the new aircraft were lost on the ground and it was again the Hurricane that bore the brunt of the early fighting until further reinforcements arrived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bader_147-155_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-bader_147-155-115">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_defence_in_Russia">Air defence in Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Air defence in Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:No._151_Wing_Royal_Air_Force_Operations_in_Russia,_September-november_1941_CR38.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1772" data-file-width="2371" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/No._151_Wing_Royal_Air_Force_Operations_in_Russia%2C_September-november_1941_CR38.jpg/220px-No._151_Wing_Royal_Air_Force_Operations_in_Russia%2C_September-november_1941_CR38.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/No._151_Wing_Royal_Air_Force_Operations_in_Russia%2C_September-november_1941_CR38.jpg/330px-No._151_Wing_Royal_Air_Force_Operations_in_Russia%2C_September-november_1941_CR38.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/No._151_Wing_Royal_Air_Force_Operations_in_Russia%2C_September-november_1941_CR38.jpg/440px-No._151_Wing_Royal_Air_Force_Operations_in_Russia%2C_September-november_1941_CR38.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:No._151_Wing_Royal_Air_Force_Operations_in_Russia,_September-november_1941_CR38.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane Mark IIB of No. 81 Squadron RAF at Murmansk-Vaenga airfield, Russia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hawker Hurricane was the first Allied <a href="/wiki/Lend-Lease" title="Lend-Lease">Lend-Lease</a> aircraft to be delivered to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> with a total of 2,952 Hurricanes eventually delivered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_p._55._117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan_p._55.-117">[109]</a></sup> becoming the most numerous British aircraft in <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[110]</a></sup> Many Soviet pilots were disappointed by the Hawker fighter, regarding it as inferior to both German and Russian aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_p._55._117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan_p._55.-117">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drabkin_p._11._119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drabkin_p._11.-119">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 1941, Mk II Hurricanes played an important air defence role when the Soviet Union found itself under threat from the approaching German Army, who were advancing across a broad front stretching from <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Leningrad</a>, Moscow, and to the oil fields in the south. Britain's decision to aid the Soviets meant sending supplies by sea to the far northern ports, and as the convoys would need to sail within range of enemy air attack from the Luftwaffe based in neighbouring Finland, it was decided to deliver a number of Hurricane Mk IIBs, flying with <a href="/wiki/No._81_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 81 Squadron RAF">Nos. 81</a> and <a href="/wiki/No._134_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 134 Squadron RAF">134 Squadrons</a> of <a href="/wiki/No._151_Wing_RAF" title="No. 151 Wing RAF">No. 151 Wing RAF</a>, to provide protection. Twenty-four were transported on the <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">carrier</a> <i><a href="/wiki/HMS_Argus_(I49)" title="HMS Argus (I49)">Argus</a></i>, arriving just off <a href="/wiki/Murmansk" title="Murmansk">Murmansk</a> on 28 August 1941, and another 15 crated aircraft on board merchant vessels. In addition to their convoy protection duties, the aircraft also acted as escorts to Russian bombers.</p>
<p>Enemy attention to the area declined in October, at which point the RAF pilots trained their Soviet counterparts to operate the Hurricanes themselves. By the end of the year, the RAF's direct role in the region had ended, but the aircraft themselves remained behind and became the first of thousands of Allied aircraft that were accepted by the Soviet Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup> Although Soviet pilots were not universally enthusiastic about the Hurricane, twice <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Hero of the Soviet Union">Hero of the Soviet Union</a> Lt. Col. <a href="/wiki/Boris_Safonov" title="Boris Safonov">Boris Safonov</a> "loved the Hurricane", and RAF Hurricane Mk IIB fighters operating from Soviet soil in defence of Murmansk, destroyed 15 Luftwaffe aircraft for only one loss in combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs_pp._84.E2.80.9385_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs_pp._84.E2.80.9385-121">[113]</a></sup> However, in some Soviet war memoirs, the Hurricane has been described in very unflattering terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The "Soviet" IIB Hurricane as a multi-role fighter-bomber had quite a few drawbacks. First of all, it was 40–50 km/h (25/31 mph) slower than its main opponent, the Bf 109E interceptor, at low and medium height, and had a slower rate of climb. The Messerschmitt could outdive the Hurricane because of the thicker wing profile of the British fighter. But the main source of complaints was the Hurricane's armament. On occasion, the eight or 12 small-calibre machine guns did not damage the sturdy and heavily armoured German aircraft; consequently, Soviet ground crews started to remove the Brownings. Retaining only four or six of the 12 machine guns, two 12.7 mm <a href="/wiki/Berezin_UB" title="Berezin UB">Berezin UBs</a> or two or even four 20 mm <a href="/wiki/ShVAK_cannon" title="ShVAK cannon">ShVAK cannons</a> were substituted, but overall performance deteriorated as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[N 10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Burma,_Ceylon,_Singapore,_and_the_Netherlands_East_Indies"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Burma.2C_Ceylon.2C_Singapore.2C_and_the_Netherlands_East_Indies">Burma, Ceylon, Singapore, and the Netherlands East Indies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Burma, Ceylon, Singapore, and the Netherlands East Indies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_singapore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="452" data-file-width="590" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Hurricane_singapore.jpg/220px-Hurricane_singapore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Hurricane_singapore.jpg/330px-Hurricane_singapore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Hurricane_singapore.jpg/440px-Hurricane_singapore.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_singapore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hawker Hurricane Mk.II of <a href="/wiki/No._232_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 232 Squadron RAF">232 Squadron</a> shot down on 8 February 1942 during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Singapore" title="Battle of Singapore">Battle of Singapore</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">war with Japan</a>, 51 Hurricane Mk IIBs were disassembled and sent in crates to <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>; these and the 24 pilots (many of whom were veterans of the Battle of Britain), who had been transferred to the theatre, formed the nucleus of five squadrons. They arrived on 3 January 1942, by which time the Allied fighter squadrons in Singapore, flying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brewster_Buffalo" title="Brewster Buffalo">Brewster Buffalos</a>, had been overwhelmed during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Malaya" title="Battle of Malaya">Malayan campaign</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Army_Air_Force" title="Imperial Japanese Army Air Force">Imperial Japanese Army Air Force</a>'s fighter force, especially the <a href="/wiki/Nakajima_Ki-43" title="Nakajima Ki-43">Nakajima Ki-43</a> Oscar, had been underestimated in its capability, numbers and the strategy of its commanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurricanes_over_Singapore_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hurricanes_over_Singapore-127">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_17_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-mason_17-128">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thanks to the efforts of the 151st Maintenance unit, the 51 Hurricanes were assembled and ready for testing within 48 hours, and of these, 21 were ready for operational service within three days. The Hurricanes were fitted with bulky 'Vokes' dust filters under the nose and were armed with 12, rather than eight, machine guns. The additional weight and drag made them slow to climb and unwieldy to manoeuvre at altitude, although they were more effective bomber killers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>The recently arrived pilots were formed into <a href="/wiki/No._232_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 232 Squadron RAF">232 Squadron</a>. In addition, <a href="/wiki/No._488_Squadron_RNZAF" title="No. 488 Squadron RNZAF">488(NZ) Squadron</a>, a Buffalo squadron, converted to Hurricanes. On 18 January, the two squadrons formed the basis of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=No._226_Group_RAF&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="No. 226 Group RAF (page does not exist)">226 Group</a>. 232 Squadron became operational on 22 January and suffered the first losses and victories for the Hurricane in Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Your_Planes_and_Your_Work_Defend_Your_Empire_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Your_Planes_and_Your_Work_Defend_Your_Empire-130">[120]</a></sup> Between 27 and 30 January, another 48 Hurricanes (Mk IIA) arrived with the aircraft carrier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HMS_Indomitable_(R92)" title="HMS Indomitable (R92)">HMS <i>Indomitable</i></a>, from which they flew to airfields code-named P1 and P2, near <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">Palembang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Netherlands_East_Indies" title="Netherlands East Indies">Netherlands East Indies</a>.</p>
<p>Because of inadequate early warning systems (the first British radar stations became operational only towards the end of February), Japanese air raids were able to destroy 30 Hurricanes on the ground in Sumatra, most of them in one raid on 7 February. After Japanese <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Singapore" title="Battle of Singapore">landings in Singapore</a>, on 10 February, the remnants of 232 and 488 Squadrons were withdrawn to Palembang. However, Japanese paratroopers began the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Palembang" title="Battle of Palembang">invasion of Sumatra</a> on 13 February. Hurricanes destroyed six Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transport_ship" title="Transport ship">transport ships</a> on 14 February, but lost seven aircraft in the process. On 18 February, the remaining Allied aircraft and aircrews moved to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Java_(island)" title="Java (island)">Java</a>. By this time, only 18 serviceable Hurricanes remained out of the original 99.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Derry_and_Robinson.2C_p._27_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Derry_and_Robinson.2C_p._27-131">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>That same month, 12 Hurricane Mk IIB Trops were supplied to the Dutch forces on Java. With dust filters removed and fuel and ammo load in wings halved, these were able to stay in a turn with the Oscars they fought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Het_Verlies_van_Java.2C_p_83_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Het_Verlies_van_Java.2C_p_83-132">[122]</a></sup> After <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Java" title="Battle of Java">Java was invaded</a>, some of the New Zealand pilots were evacuated by sea to Australia. One aircraft which had not been assembled, was transferred to the RAAF, becoming the only Hurricane to see service in Australia, in training and other non-combat units.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RAF_Hurricane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="639" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/RAF_Hurricane.jpg/220px-RAF_Hurricane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/RAF_Hurricane.jpg/330px-RAF_Hurricane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/RAF_Hurricane.jpg/440px-RAF_Hurricane.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RAF_Hurricane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane <i>V7476</i>, which was transferred from Singapore and was the only Hurricane based in Australia during the Second World War. Note the tropicalised Vokes air filter which was fitted to many types operating in the Pacific.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When a Japanese carrier task force under the command of Admiral <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%ABichi_Nagumo" title="Chūichi Nagumo">Chūichi Nagumo</a> made a <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_raid" title="Indian Ocean raid">sortie into the Indian Ocean</a> in April 1942, RAF Hurricanes based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a> saw action against Nagumo's forces during attacks on <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a> on 5 April 1942 and on <a href="/wiki/Trincomalee" title="Trincomalee">Trincomalee</a> harbour on 9 April 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p392-3.2C5.2C9_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p392-3.2C5.2C9-133">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 5 April 1942, Captain <a href="/wiki/Mitsuo_Fuchida" title="Mitsuo Fuchida">Mitsuo Fuchida</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">Imperial Japanese Navy</a>, who led the <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a>, led a strike against Colombo with 53 <a href="/wiki/Nakajima_B5N" title="Nakajima B5N">Nakajima B5N</a> torpedo bombers and 38 <a href="/wiki/Aichi_D3A" title="Aichi D3A">Aichi D3A</a> dive bombers, escorted by 36 <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_A6M_Zero" title="Mitsubishi A6M Zero">Mitsubishi A6M Zero</a> fighters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p395_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p395-134">[124]</a></sup> They were opposed by 35 Hurricane I and IIBs of <a href="/wiki/No._30_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 30 Squadron RAF">30</a> and <a href="/wiki/No._258_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 258 Squadron RAF">258 Squadrons</a>, together with six <a href="/wiki/Fairey_Fulmar" title="Fairey Fulmar">Fairey Fulmars</a> of <a href="/wiki/803_Naval_Air_Squadron" title="803 Naval Air Squadron">803</a> and <a href="/wiki/806_Naval_Air_Squadron" title="806 Naval Air Squadron">806</a> Squadrons of the Fleet Air Arm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p397_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p397-135">[125]</a></sup> The Hurricanes mainly tried to shoot down the attacking bombers, but were engaged heavily by the escorting Zeros.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vac_p39_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vac_p39-136">[126]</a></sup> A total of 21 Hurricanes were shot down (although two of these were repairable),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p403_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p403-137">[127]</a></sup> together with four Fulmars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p397-8_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p397-8-138">[128]</a></sup> and six Swordfish of <a href="/wiki/788_Naval_Air_Squadron" title="788 Naval Air Squadron">788 Naval Air Squadron</a> that had been surprised in flight by the raid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p395-7_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p395-7-139">[129]</a></sup> The RAF claimed 18 Japanese aircraft destroyed, seven probably destroyed and nine damaged, with one aircraft claimed by a Fulmar and five by anti-aircraft fire. This compared with actual Japanese losses of one Zero and six D3As, with a further seven D3As, five B5Ns and three Zeros damaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vac_p39_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vac_p39-136">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p403-4_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p403-4-140">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 9 April 1942, the Japanese task force sent 91 B5Ns escorted by 41 Zeros against Trincomalee port and the nearby <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SLAF_China_Bay" title="SLAF China Bay">China Bay airfield</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p413_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p413-141">[131]</a></sup> A total of 16 Hurricanes opposed the raid, of which eight were lost with a further three damaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p420_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p420-142">[132]</a></sup> They claimed eight Japanese aircraft destroyed with a further four probably destroyed and at least five damaged. Actual Japanese losses were three A6Ms and two B5Ns, with a further 10 B5Ns damaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodyv2_p421-2_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodyv2_p421-2-143">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epilogue">Epilogue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Epilogue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The battles over the <a href="/wiki/Rakhine_State" title="Rakhine State">Arakan</a> in 1943 represented the last large-scale use of the Hurricane as a pure day fighter. But they were still used in the fighter-bomber role in Burma until the end of the war and they were occasionally caught up in air combat as well. For example, on 15 February 1944, Flg Off Jagadish Chandra Verma of No 6 Sqdn of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Indian_Air_Force" title="Royal Indian Air Force">Royal Indian Air Force</a> shot down a Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ki-43_Oscar" title="Ki-43 Oscar">Ki-43 Oscar</a>: it was the only RIAF victory of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_2003.2C_p._81_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_2003.2C_p._81-144">[134]</a></sup> The Hurricane remained in service as a fighter-bomber over the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> and at home as well where it was used mainly for second-line tasks and occasionally flown by ace pilots. For example, in mid-1944, ace Sqdn Leader 'Jas' Storrar flew No 1687 Hurricane to deliver priority mail to Allied armies in France during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normandy_invasion" title="Normandy invasion">Normandy invasion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_2003.2C_p._81_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_2003.2C_p._81-144">[134]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Sea_Hurricanes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="800" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Hawker_Sea_Hurricanes.jpg/220px-Hawker_Sea_Hurricanes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Hawker_Sea_Hurricanes.jpg/330px-Hawker_Sea_Hurricanes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Hawker_Sea_Hurricanes.jpg/440px-Hawker_Sea_Hurricanes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Sea_Hurricanes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sea Hurricane Mk IB in formation, December 1941</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft_carrier_operations">Aircraft carrier operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Aircraft carrier operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane_variants" title="Hawker Hurricane variants">Hawker Hurricane variants</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane_variants" title="Hawker Hurricane variants">Sea Hurricane</a> became operational in mid-1941 and scored its first kill while operating from <a href="/wiki/HMS_Furious_(47)" title="HMS Furious (47)">HMS <i>Furious</i></a> on 31 July 1941. During the next three years, <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a> Sea Hurricanes were to feature prominently while operating from Royal Navy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aircraft_carriers" title="Aircraft carriers">aircraft carriers</a>. The Sea Hurricane scored an impressive kill-to-loss ratio,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[N 11]</a></sup> primarily while defending <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malta_Convoys" title="Malta Convoys">Malta convoys</a>, and operating from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Escort_carriers" title="Escort carriers">escort carriers</a> in the Atlantic Ocean. As an example, on 26 May 1944, Royal Navy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_Hurricane" title="Sea Hurricane">Sea Hurricanes</a> operating from the escort carrier <a href="/wiki/HMS_Nairana_(D05)" title="HMS Nairana (D05)">HMS <i>Nairana</i></a> claimed the destruction of three <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_290" title="Junkers Ju 290">Ju 290</a> reconnaissance aircraft during the defence of a convoy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hurricane_aces">Hurricane aces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hurricane aces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The top scoring Hurricane pilot was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squadron_Leader" title="Squadron Leader">Squadron Leader</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marmaduke_Pattle" title="Marmaduke Pattle">Marmaduke "Pat" Pattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Flying_Cross_(United_Kingdom)" title="Distinguished Flying Cross (United Kingdom)">DFC</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Medal_bar" title="Medal bar">Bar</a>, with 35 Hawker fighter victories (out of career 50 total, with two shared) serving with No. 80 and 33 Squadrons. All of his Hurricane kills were achieved over Greece in 1941. He was shot down and killed in the Battle of Athens. Wing Commander <a href="/wiki/Frank_Reginald_Carey" title="Frank Reginald Carey">Frank Reginald Carey</a> claimed 28 air victories while flying Hurricanes during 1939–43, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squadron_Leader" title="Squadron Leader">Squadron Leader</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Vale" title="William Vale">William "Cherry" Vale</a> <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Flying_Cross_(United_Kingdom)" title="Distinguished Flying Cross (United Kingdom)">DFC</a> <a href="/wiki/Medal_bar" title="Medal bar">and Bar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Cross_(United_Kingdom)" title="Air Force Cross (United Kingdom)">AFC</a> totalled 20 kills (of 30) in Greece and Syria with No. 80 Squadron. Czech pilot F/Lt <a href="/wiki/Karel_Kuttelwascher" title="Karel Kuttelwascher">Karel M. Kuttelwascher</a> achieved all of his 18 air victories with the Hurricane, most as an intruder night fighter with No. 1 Squadron. Pilot Officer V.C. Woodward (33 and 213 Squadrons) was another top-scoring ace with 14 (out of 18 total, three of which are shared), while F/Lt <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_P._Stevens&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Richard P. Stevens (page does not exist)">Richard P. Stevens</a> claimed all of his 14.5 enemy aircraft flying the Hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomas_p.83_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tomas_p.83-148">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_John_Cork" title="Richard John Cork">Richard "Dickie" Cork</a> was the leading <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a> Sea Hurricane ace, with nine destroyed, two shared, one probable, four damaged and seven destroyed on the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[138]</a></sup> Czech pilot <a href="/wiki/Josef_Franti%C5%A1ek" title="Josef František">Josef František</a>, flying with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/303_Polish_Squadron" title="303 Polish Squadron">303 Polish Squadron</a>, shot down at least 17 enemy aircraft over southeast England during September–October 1940. Polish pilot <a href="/wiki/Witold_Urbanowicz" title="Witold Urbanowicz">Witold Urbanowicz</a>, flying with 303 Polish Squadron, had 15 confirmed kills and one probable during the Battle of Britain.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane_variants" title="Hawker Hurricane variants">Hawker Hurricane variants</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_F2344A.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="597" data-file-width="800" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_F2344A.jpg/220px-Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_F2344A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_F2344A.jpg/330px-Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_F2344A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_F2344A.jpg/440px-Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_F2344A.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricanes_at_Lille-Seclin_-_Royal_Air_Force_1939-1945_Fighter_Command_F2344A.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Mk Is in France with original two-bladed Watts propellers</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk I</dt>
<dd>First production version, with fabric-covered wings, a wooden two-bladed, fixed-<a href="/wiki/Blade_pitch" title="Blade pitch">pitch</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propeller_(aircraft)" title="Propeller (aircraft)">propeller</a>, powered by the 1,030 hp (770 kW) <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Limited" title="Rolls-Royce Limited">Rolls-Royce</a> <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">Merlin</a> Mk II or III engines and armed with eight <a href="/wiki/.303_British" title=".303 British">.303 in</a> (7.7 mm) <a href="/wiki/M1919_Browning_machine_gun" title="M1919 Browning machine gun">Browning machine guns</a>. Produced between 1937 and 1939.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk I (revised)</dt>
<dd>A revised Hurricane Mk I series built with a <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland" title="De Havilland">de Havilland</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dowty_Rotol" title="Dowty Rotol">Rotol</a> constant speed metal propeller, metal-covered wings, armour and other improvements. In 1939, the RAF had taken on about 500 of this later design to form the backbone of the fighter squadrons.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk IIA Series 1</dt>
<dd>Hurricane Mk I powered by the improved Merlin XX engine with two-speed supercharger. This new engine used a mix of 30 per cent glycol and 70 per cent water. Pure glycol is flammable, so not only was the new mix safer, but the engine also ran approximately 21 °C (70 °F) cooler, which gave longer engine life and greater reliability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlaiefer_p._220_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schlaiefer_p._220-150">[139]</a></sup> The new engine was longer than the earlier Merlin and so the Hurricane gained a 4.5 in "plug" in front of the cockpit, which made the aircraft slightly more stable due to the slight forward shift in centre of gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._16_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._16-151">[140]</a></sup> First flew on 11 June 1940 and went into squadron service in September 1940.</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="778" data-file-width="1173" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Hurricane.jpg/220px-Hurricane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Hurricane.jpg/330px-Hurricane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Hurricane.jpg/440px-Hurricane.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hawker Hurricane Mk IIB <i>Z5140</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk IIB (Hurricane IIA Series 2)</dt>
<dd>The Hurricane II B were fitted with racks allowing them to carry two 250 lb or two 500 lb bombs. This lowered the top speed of the Hurricane to 301 mph (484 km/h), but by this point mixed sweeps of Hurricanes carrying bombs, protected by a screen of fighter Hurricanes were not uncommon. The same racks allowed the Hurricane to carry two 45-gallon (205 l) drop tanks instead of the bombs, more than doubling the Hurricane's fuel load.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._18_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._18-152">[141]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>Hurricane Mk IIA Series 2 was equipped with new and slightly longer propeller spinner and new wing-mounting 12 x .303 in (7.7 mm) Browning machine guns. The first aircraft were built in October 1940 and were renamed <b>Mark IIB</b> in April 1941.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk IIB Trop.</dt>
<dd>For use in North Africa the Hawker Hurricane Mk IIB (and other variants) were tropicalised. They were fitted with Vokes and Rolls-Royce engine dust filters and the pilots were issued with a desert survival kit, including a bottle of water behind the cockpit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._19_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._19-153">[142]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_IIC_3_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_c1942.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="595" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Hurricane_IIC_3_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_c1942.jpg/220px-Hurricane_IIC_3_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_c1942.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Hurricane_IIC_3_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_c1942.jpg/330px-Hurricane_IIC_3_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_c1942.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Hurricane_IIC_3_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_c1942.jpg/440px-Hurricane_IIC_3_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_c1942.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_IIC_3_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_c1942.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane Mk IIC <i>BD867</i> of 3 Sqn RAF, 1942</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk IIC (Hurricane Mk IIA Series 2)</dt>
<dd>Hurricane Mk IIA Series 1 equipped with new and slightly longer propeller spinner, and fully replaced the machine-gun armament with four 20 mm (.79 in) <a href="/wiki/Hispano-Suiza_HS.404" title="Hispano-Suiza HS.404">Hispano Mk II</a> cannons, two per side. Hurricane IIA Series 2 became the Mk IIC in June 1941, using a slightly modified wing. The new wings also included a hardpoint for a 500 or 250 lb (230 or 110 kg) bomb and, later in 1941, fuel tanks. By then performance was inferior to the latest German fighters, and the Hurricane changed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_attack_aircraft" title="Ground attack aircraft">ground-attack</a> role, sometimes referred to as the <b>Hurribomber</b>. The mark also served as a <a href="/wiki/Night_fighter" title="Night fighter">night fighter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intruder_(air_combat)" title="Intruder (air combat)">intruder</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk IID</dt>
<dd>Hurricane Mk IIB conversion armed with two 40 mm (1.57 in) anti-tank autocannons in a <a href="/wiki/Gun_pod" title="Gun pod">gondola-style pod</a>, one under each wing and a single Browning machine gun in each wing loaded with tracers for aiming purposes. The first aircraft flew on 18 September 1941 and deliveries started in 1942. Serial-built aircraft had additional armour for the pilot, radiator and engine, and were armed with a Rolls-Royce gun with 12 rounds, later changed to the 40 mm (1.57 in) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vickers_S_gun" title="Vickers S gun">Vickers S gun</a> with 15 rounds. The outer wing attachments were strengthened so that 4G could be pulled at a weight of 8,540 lb (3,874 kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._17_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._17-154">[143]</a></sup> The weight of guns and armour protection marginally impaired the aircraft's performance. These Hurricanes were nicknamed "Flying Can Openers", perhaps a play on the logo of No. 6 Squadron, which flew the Hurricane starting in 1941.</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_IID_6_Sqn_RAF_over_Western_Desert_1942.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="349" data-file-width="473" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hurricane_IID_6_Sqn_RAF_over_Western_Desert_1942.jpg/220px-Hurricane_IID_6_Sqn_RAF_over_Western_Desert_1942.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hurricane_IID_6_Sqn_RAF_over_Western_Desert_1942.jpg/330px-Hurricane_IID_6_Sqn_RAF_over_Western_Desert_1942.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hurricane_IID_6_Sqn_RAF_over_Western_Desert_1942.jpg/440px-Hurricane_IID_6_Sqn_RAF_over_Western_Desert_1942.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_IID_6_Sqn_RAF_over_Western_Desert_1942.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane IID of 6 Sqn showing Vokes tropical filter and RAF desert camouflage in 1942.</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk IIE</dt>
<dd>Another wing modification was introduced in the <b>Mk IIE</b>, but the changes became extensive enough that it was renamed the <b>Mk IV</b> after the first 250 had been delivered.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk T.IIC</dt>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Two-seat_Hawker_Hurricane_of_Air_Force_of_Iran.jpg"><img alt="A Two-seat Hawker Hurricane of Air Force of Iran.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="305" data-file-width="499" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/A_Two-seat_Hawker_Hurricane_of_Air_Force_of_Iran.jpg/220px-A_Two-seat_Hawker_Hurricane_of_Air_Force_of_Iran.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/A_Two-seat_Hawker_Hurricane_of_Air_Force_of_Iran.jpg/330px-A_Two-seat_Hawker_Hurricane_of_Air_Force_of_Iran.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/A_Two-seat_Hawker_Hurricane_of_Air_Force_of_Iran.jpg/440px-A_Two-seat_Hawker_Hurricane_of_Air_Force_of_Iran.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Two-seat_Hawker_Hurricane_of_Air_Force_of_Iran.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>Two-seat training version of the Mk. IIC. Only two aircraft were built, for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Iranian_Air_Force" title="Imperial Iranian Air Force">Imperial Iranian Air Force</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk III</dt>
<dd>Version of the Hurricane Mk II powered by a US <a href="/wiki/Packard" title="Packard">Packard</a>-built Merlin engine, intending to provide supplies of the British-built engines for other designs. By the time production was to have started, British Merlin production had increased to the point where the idea was abandoned.</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_Mark_IV.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="800" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hawker_Hurricane_Mark_IV.jpg/220px-Hawker_Hurricane_Mark_IV.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hawker_Hurricane_Mark_IV.jpg/330px-Hawker_Hurricane_Mark_IV.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hawker_Hurricane_Mark_IV.jpg/440px-Hawker_Hurricane_Mark_IV.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_Mark_IV.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane Mk IV, armed with RP-3 rockets</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk IV</dt>
<dd>The last major change to the Hurricane was the introduction of the "universal Wing", a single design able to mount two 250 or 500 lb (110 or 230 kg) bombs, two 40 mm (1.57 in) Vickers S guns, drop tanks or eight <a href="/wiki/RP-3" title="RP-3">"60 pounder" RP-3</a> rockets. Two .303 in Brownings were fitted to aid aiming of the heavier armament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mason_Hawker_p285_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mason_Hawker_p285-155">[144]</a></sup> The new design also incorporated the improved Merlin 24 or 27 engines of 1,620 hp (1,210 kW), equipped with dust filters for desert operations. The Merlin 27 had a redesigned oil system that was better suited to operations in the tropics, and which was rated at a slightly lower altitude in keeping with the Hurricane's new role as a close-support fighter. The radiator was deeper and armoured. Additional armour was also fitted around the engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._20_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._20-156">[145]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk V</dt>
<dd>The final variant to be produced. Only three were built, and the variant never reached production. This was powered by a Merlin 32 boosted engine to give 1,700 hp at low level and was intended as a dedicated ground-attack aircraft to use in <a href="/wiki/Burma_Campaign" title="Burma Campaign">Burma</a>. All three prototypes had four-bladed propellers. Speed was 326 mph (525 km/h) at 500 ft, which is comparable with the Hurricane I despite being one and a half times as heavy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._20_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._20-156">[145]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk X</dt>
<dd>Canadian-built variant. Single-seat fighter and fighter-bomber. Powered by a 1,300 hp (970 kW) Packard Merlin 28. Eight 0.303 in (7.7 mm) machine guns mounted in the wings. In total, 490 were built.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk XI</dt>
<dd>Canadian-built variant. 150 were built.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk XII</dt>
<dd>Canadian-built variant. Single-seat fighter and fighter-bomber. Powered by a 1,300 hp (969 kW) Packard Merlin 29. Initially armed with 12 0.303 in (7.7 mm) machine guns, but this was later changed to four 20 mm (.79 in) cannon.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Mk XIIA</dt>
<dd>Canadian-built variant. Single-seat fighter and fighter-bomber. Powered by a 1,300 hp (970 kW) Packard Merlin 29, armed with eight 0.303 in (7.7 mm) machine guns.</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Royal_Navy_during_the_Second_World_War_A9421.jpg"><img alt="The Royal Navy during the Second World War A9421.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="636" data-file-width="800" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/The_Royal_Navy_during_the_Second_World_War_A9421.jpg/220px-The_Royal_Navy_during_the_Second_World_War_A9421.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/The_Royal_Navy_during_the_Second_World_War_A9421.jpg/330px-The_Royal_Navy_during_the_Second_World_War_A9421.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/The_Royal_Navy_during_the_Second_World_War_A9421.jpg/440px-The_Royal_Navy_during_the_Second_World_War_A9421.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Royal_Navy_during_the_Second_World_War_A9421.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>Sea Hurricane Mk IA</dt>
<dd>The Sea Hurricane Mk IA was a Hurricane Mk I modified by <a href="/wiki/General_Aircraft_Limited" title="General Aircraft Limited">General Aircraft Limited</a>. These conversions numbered approximately 250 aircraft. They were modified to be carried by <a href="/wiki/CAM_ship" title="CAM ship">CAM ships</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aircraft_catapult" title="Aircraft catapult">catapult</a>-armed <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">merchantman</a>), whose ships' crews were Merchant Marine and whose Hurricanes were crewed and serviced by RAF personnel, or Fighter Catapult Ships, which were Naval Auxiliary Vessels crewed by naval personnel and aircraft operated by the Fleet Air Arm. These ships were equipped with a catapult for launching an aircraft, but without facilities to recover them. Consequently, if the aircraft were not in range of a land base, pilots had to bail out or to ditch.</dd>
<dd>Both of these options had their problems—there was always a chance of striking part of the fuselage when bailing out, and a number of pilots had been killed in this way. Ditching the Hurricane in the sea called for skill as the radiator housing acted as a water brake, pitching the nose of the fighter downwards when it hit the water, while also acting as a very efficient scoop, helping to flood the Hurricane so that a quick exit was necessary before the aircraft sank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._20_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._20-156">[145]</a></sup> Then the pilot had to be picked up by a ship. More than 80 modifications were needed to convert a Hurricane into a Sea Hurricane, including new radios to conform with those used by the Fleet Air Arm and new instrumentation to read in knots rather than miles per hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._19_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._19-153">[142]</a></sup> They were informally known as "Hurricats".</dd>
<dd>The majority of the aircraft modified had suffered wear-and-tear serving with front line squadrons, so much so that at least one example used during trials broke up under the stress of a catapult launching. CAM Sea Hurricanes were launched operationally on eight occasions and the Hurricanes shot down six enemy aircraft for the loss of one Hurricane pilot killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown.2C_Eric_p112_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown.2C_Eric_p112-157">[146]</a></sup> The first Sea Hurricane IA kill was an <a href="/wiki/Focke-Wulf_Fw_200_Condor" title="Focke-Wulf Fw 200 Condor">FW 200C <i>Condor</i></a>, shot down on 2 August 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[147]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="859" data-file-width="1985" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Hawker_Hurricane03.jpg/220px-Hawker_Hurricane03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Hawker_Hurricane03.jpg/330px-Hawker_Hurricane03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Hawker_Hurricane03.jpg/440px-Hawker_Hurricane03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Preserved Sea Hurricane of the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>Sea Hurricane Mk IB</dt>
<dd>Hurricane Mk I version equipped with catapult spools plus an arrester hook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown.2C_Eric_p114_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown.2C_Eric_p114-159">[148]</a></sup> From July 1941 they operated from <a href="/wiki/HMS_Furious_(47)" title="HMS Furious (47)">HMS <i>Furious</i></a> and from October 1941, they were used on <a href="/wiki/Merchant_aircraft_carrier" title="Merchant aircraft carrier">merchant aircraft carrier</a> (MAC) ships, which were large cargo vessels with a flight deck fitted, enabling aircraft to be launched and recovered. A total of 340 aircraft were converted. The first Sea Hurricane IB kill occurred on 31 July 1941 when Sea Hurricanes of 880 squadron FAA operating from HMS <i>Furious</i> shot down a <a href="/wiki/Dornier_Do_18" title="Dornier Do 18">Do 18 flying-boat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[149]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Sea Hurricane Mk IC</dt>
<dd>Hurricane Mk I<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown.2C_Eric_p114_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown.2C_Eric_p114-159">[148]</a></sup> version equipped with catapult spools, an arrester hook and the four-cannon wing. From February 1942, 400 aircraft were converted. The Sea Hurricane IC used during <i><a href="/wiki/Operation_Pedestal" title="Operation Pedestal">Operation Pedestal</a></i> had their Merlin III engines modified to accept 16 lb boost, and could generate more than 1,400 hp at low altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[151]</a></sup> Lt. R. J. Cork was credited with five kills while flying a Sea Hurricane IC during <i>Operation Pedestal</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[152]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Sea Hurricane Mk IIC</dt>
<dd>Hurricane Mk IIC version equipped with naval radio gear; 400 aircraft were converted and used on fleet carriers. The Merlin XX engine on the Sea Hurricane generated 1,460 hp at 6,250 ft and 1,435 hp at 11,000 ft. Top speed was 322 mph at 13,500ft and 342 mph at 22,000 ft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown.2C_Eric_p112_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown.2C_Eric_p112-157">[146]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Sea Hurricane Mk XIIA</dt>
<dd>Canadian-built Hurricane Mk XIIA converted into Sea Hurricanes.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hillson F.40 (a.k.a. F.H.40)</dt>
<dd>A full-scale version of the Hills &amp; Son <a href="/wiki/Hillson_Bi-mono" title="Hillson Bi-mono">Bi-mono</a> slip-wing Biplane/monoplane, using a Hawker Hurricane Mk I returned from Canada as RCAF ser no 321 (RAF serial L1884). Taxi and flight trials carried out at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAF_Sealand" title="RAF Sealand">RAF Sealand</a> during May 1943, and at the <a href="/wiki/Aeroplane_and_Armament_Experimental_Establishment" title="Aeroplane and Armament Experimental Establishment">Aeroplane and Armament Experimental Establishment</a>, Boscombe Down from September 1943. The upper wing was not released in flight before the programme was terminated due to poor performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarret_AM_p18-3_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jarret_AM_p18-3-164">[153]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Photo Reconnaissance</dt>
<dd>The Service Depot at Heliopolis in Egypt converted several Hurricanes Is for photo reconnaissance. The first three were converted in January 1941. Two carried a pair of <a href="/wiki/F24_camera" title="F24 camera">F24 cameras</a> with 8-inch focal length lenses. The third carried one vertical and two oblique F24s with 14-inch focal length lenses mounted in the rear fuselage, close to the trailing edge of the wing, and a fairing was built up over the lenses aft of the radiator housing. A further five Hurricanes were modified in March 1941, and two were converted in a similar manner in Malta during April 1941. During October 1941 a batch of six Hurricane IIs was converted to PR Mark II status and a final batch, thought to be of 12 aircraft, was converted in late 1941. The PR Mark II was said to be capable of slightly over 350 mph (560 km/h) and was able to reach 38,000 ft (12,000 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._18_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._18-152">[141]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Hurricane Tac R</dt>
<dd>For duties closer to the front lines some Hurricanes were converted to Tactical Reconnaissance (Tac R) aircraft. An additional radio was fitted for liaison with ground forces who were better placed to direct the Hurricane. Some Hurricane Tac R aircraft also had a vertical camera fitted in the rear fuselage, so to compensate for the extra weight either one or two Brownings or two cannons would be omitted. Externally these aircraft were only distinguishable by the missing armament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiscock_p._18_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hiscock_p._18-152">[141]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operators">Operators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Hurricane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Operators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yu_Hurricane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Yu_Hurricane.jpg/220px-Yu_Hurricane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Yu_Hurricane.jpg/330px-Yu_Hurricane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Yu_Hurricane.jpg/440px-Yu_Hurricane.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yu_Hurricane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hawker Hurricane Mk IVRP with Yugoslav Air Force markings, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museum_of_Aviation_in_Belgrade" title="Museum of Aviation in Belgrade">Museum of Aviation in Belgrade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgrade" title="Belgrade">Belgrade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Hawker_Hurricane_operators" title="List of Hawker Hurricane operators">List of Hawker Hurricane operators</a></div>
<p>Due to its lightweight, yet robust, construction and ease of maintenance, the Hurricane had a long operational life in many theatres of war. It was also built by, or exported to, several other countries. The Hurricane was unusual in that it was flown operationally by both the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_Powers_of_World_War_II" title="Axis Powers of World War II">Axis</a> during the war. In some cases (e.g. Portugal and Ireland) the Hurricane was pressed into service after being forced to land in a neutral country.</p>
<p>In 1939 <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> ordered 30 Hurricane fighters and paid for them. However, due to the start of the Second World War in September 1939, the aircraft were never delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[154]</a></sup></p>
<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
